Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)      

# Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)

<span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author"><span class="ltx_personname">Haoyu Han<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id1.1.id1">1</sup>, Yu Wang<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id2.2.id2">2</sup><span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnotemark" id="footnotex1"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_note_type">footnotemark: </span><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">1</span></span></span></span>, Harry Shomer<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id3.3.id3">1</sup>, Kai Guo<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id4.4.id4">1</sup>, Jiayuan Ding<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id5.5.id5">5</sup>, Yongjia Lei<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id6.6.id6">2</sup>,<br class="ltx_break"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="id7.7.id7">Mahantesh Halappanavar<sup class="ltx_sup" id="id7.7.id7.1">3</sup>, Ryan A. ###### Abstract

Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) is a powerful technique that enhances downstream task execution by retrieving additional information, such as knowledge, skills, and tools from external sources. Graph, by its intrinsic "nodes connected by edges" nature, encodes massive heterogeneous and relational information, making it a golden resource for RAG in tremendous real-world applications. As a result, we have recently witnessed increasing attention on equipping RAG with Graph, i.e., GraphRAG. However, unlike conventional RAG, where the retriever, generator, and external data sources can be uniformly designed in the neural-embedding space, the uniqueness of graph-structured data, such as diverse-formatted and domain-specific relational knowledge, poses unique and significant challenges when designing GraphRAG for different domains. Given the broad applicability, the associated design challenges, and the recent surge in GraphRAG, a systematic and up-to-date survey of its key concepts and techniques is urgently desired. Following this motivation, we present a comprehensive and up-to-date survey on GraphRAG. Our survey first proposes a holistic GraphRAG framework by defining its key components, including query processor, retriever, organizer, generator, and data source. Furthermore, recognizing that graphs in different domains exhibit distinct relational patterns and require dedicated designs, we review GraphRAG techniques uniquely tailored to each domain. Finally, we discuss research challenges and brainstorm directions to inspire cross-disciplinary opportunities. Our survey repository is publicly maintained at [https://github.com/Graph-RAG/GraphRAG/](https://github.com/Graph-RAG/GraphRAG/).

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">1 </span> Introduction

Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG), as a powerful technique to improve downstream tasks by retrieving additional information from external data sources, has been successfully applied to various real-world applications \[[87](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib87), [120](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib120), [514](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib514), [551](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib551)\]. In RAG frameworks, retrievers search for additional knowledge, skills, and tools based on user-defined queries or task instructions. The retrieved content is then refined by an organizer and seamlessly integrated with the original query or instruction, which is further fed into the generator to produce the final answer. For example, when conducting question-answering (QA) tasks, the classic "Retriever-then-Reader" frameworks \[[191](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib191), [196](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib196), [468](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib468), [562](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib562)\] retrieve external factual knowledge to improve the answer faithfulness, which significantly benefit social goodness and mitigate risks in high-stake scenarios (e.g., medical, legal, financial, and education consultation \[[467](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib467), [472](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib472), [515](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib515)\]). Moreover, recent advancements in large language models (LLMs) have further underscored the power of RAG in enhancing the social responsibility of LLMs, such as mitigating hallucinations \[[397](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib397)\], enhancing interpretability and transparency \[[203](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib203)\], enabling dynamic adaptability \[[360](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib360), [419](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib419)\], reducing privacy risks \[[512](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib512), [513](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib513)\], ensuring reliability/robust responses \[[105](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib105), [460](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib460)\], and promoting fair treatment \[[362](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib362)\].

Building on the unprecedented success of RAG and further considering the ubiquity of graphs in real-world applications \[[545](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib545)\], recent research has explored the integration of RAG with graph-structured data. Unlike textual or visual data, graph-structured data encodes heterogeneous and relational information through its intrinsic "nodes connected by edges" nature. For example, individuals connected by social relationships of social networks usually exhibit homophily behaviors \[[291](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib291)\], sequential decision-making steps in plans follow casual dependency \[[454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454)\], and atoms belonging to the same functional group within a molecule possess unique structural properties \[[103](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib103), [508](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib508)\]. Designing the RAG that utilizes relational information requires adapting its core components, such as the retriever and generator, to seamlessly integrate graph-structured data, resulting in GraphRAG. Different from RAG, which predominantly uses semantic/lexical similarity search \[[104](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib104), [120](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib120)\], GraphRAG offers unique advantages in capturing relational knowledge by leveraging graph-based machine learning (e.g., Graph Neural Networks (GNNs)) and graph/network analysis techniques (e.g., Graph Traversal Search and Community Detection \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\]). For example, considering the query “What drugs are used to treat epithelioid sarcoma and also affect the EZH2 gene product?" \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\], blindly executing the existing BM25 or embedding-based search that relies solely on semantic/lexical similarity ignores relational knowledge encoded in graph structure. In contrast, some GraphRAG methods traverse the graph along the relational path “Disease (Epithelioid Sarcoma) <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S1.p2.1.m1.1a"><mo id="S1.p2.1.m1.1.1" stretchy="false" xref="S1.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml">→</mo><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p2.1.m1.1b"><ci stretchy="false" xref="S1.p2.3.m3.1.1.cmml">←</mo><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S1.p2.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S1.p2.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S1.p2.3.m3.1.1">←</ci></annotation-xml><annotation alttext="\leftarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S1.p2.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S1.p2.4.m4.1a"><mo id="S1.p2.4.m4.1.1" stretchy="false" xref="S1.p2.4.m4.1.1.cmml">←</mo><annotation-xml id="S1.p2.4.m4.1c">\leftarrow</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S1.p2.4.m4.1d">←</annotation></semantics></math> Gene/Protein (EZH2 gene product)" to retrieve neighbors of Epithelioid Disease following the relation \[indication\], neighbors of Gene EZH2 following the relation \[target\], and find their intersected drug \[[186](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib186), [271](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib271), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\]. Moreover, some domains involve entities with extremely complex geometry that require dedicated model design to characterize. For example, 3D structures in molecular graphs \[[52](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib52), [445](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib445)\] and hierarchical tree structures commonly found in product taxonomies (e.g., on Amazon \[[529](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib529)\]), in document sections (e.g., when using Adobe Acrobat \[[537](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib537)\]), and social networks (e.g., at Snap \[[277](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib277)\]) requires carefully designed graph encoders (or, more precisely, geometric encoders) with appropriate expressiveness to capture structural nuances \[[277](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib277), [527](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib527)\]. Simply verbalizing node texts and feeding them into LLMs cannot express complex geometric information and becomes infeasible given the exponentially growing textual descriptions as neighborhood layers expand.

Despite the above advantages of GraphRAGs over RAGs, designing appropriate GraphRAGs faces unprecedented challenges due to the following differences in graph-structured data:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Difference 1 - Unified versus (vs.) Diverse-Formatted Information</span>: Unlike conventional RAG, where semantic information can be uniformly represented as a 2D grid of image patches or a 1D sequence of textual corpora, graph-structured data often encompass diverse formats and are stored in heterogeneous sources \[[4](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib4), [26](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib26), [434](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib434)\]. For example, document graphs embed entities as sentence chunks \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\], knowledge graphs store graph information as triplets or paths \[[38](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib38)\], and molecule graphs consist of higher-order structures (e.g., cellular complexes) \[[26](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib26)\], as shown in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S1.F1 "Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)"). Some graph data may even be multimodal (e.g., text-attributed graphs include both structural and textual attributes, and scene graphs combine structures and vision). Consequently, this diversity necessitates different RAG designs. For retrievers, conventional RAG assumes the target information is indexed in an image or text corpus, which can be uniformly represented as vector embeddings and enable one-size-fits-all embedding-based retrieval. However, retrievers for GraphRAG must consider the concrete format and source of the desired information, making the one-size-fits-all design impractical. When dealing with knowledge graph question-answering, information of nodes, edges, or subgraphs is usually fetched by graph search before embedding matching-based retrieval \[[419](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib419), [492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492)\]. This fetching operation is usually conducted by identifying relevant nodes/edges/subgraphs via entity linking, relational matching, and graph search algorithms (e.g., Breadth-First Search, Depth-First Search, Monte Carlo Tree Search, and A\* search) \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [419](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib419), [570](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib570)\], which is unachievable if solely through deep learning-based embedding similarity search. Furthermore, the design of the retriever should ensure sufficient geometric expressiveness to capture structural nuances. For instance, when retrieving APIs from a plan graph to accomplish specific goals \[[355](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib355), [356](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib356), [454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454)\], it is essential to equip the retriever with directional awareness. This enables the execution of APIs with resource dependencies in the correct order, preventing conflicts and avoiding invalid operations. Similarly, designing expressive retrievers capable of differentiating high-order subgraph structures, such as 6-cycle benzene versus vs. 4-star Methane, and 3-star T-junction vs. 4-square road, is essential in drug design for disease treatment \[[139](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib139)\] and road construction for city planning \[[209](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib209)\]. Beyond the retriever, the generator also requires specialized designs. When retrieved content includes complex graph structures with textual attributes, simply verbalizing the text of the subgraph and concatenating it into a prompt may obscure critical structural information. In these cases, encoding the graph with graph encoders such as GNNs before integrating it into generation can help preserve structural nuances \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134), [244](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib244), [434](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib434), [443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443), [456](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib456)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Difference 2 - Independent vs. Interdependent Information:</span> In conventional RAG, information is stored and utilized independently. For example, documents are split into chunks, such as individual sentences, paragraphs, or a fixed number of tokens, based on the document context and downstream task \[[21](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib21), [562](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib562)\]. Each chunk is then indexed and stored independently in a vector database. This independence prevents the retrieval from capturing chunk relations, which hinders performance on tasks requiring multi-hop reasoning and long-form planning. However, GraphRAG stores chunks as interconnected nodes with edges denoting their relations, which can benefit retrieval, organization, and generation. For retrieval, these edges could enable multi-hop traversal to capture other chunks that share a logical connection with existing retrieved chunks. Furthermore, the retrieved content can be organized not only by their semantic meaning (e.g., reranking \[[43](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib43), [172](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib172), [256](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib256)\]) but also their structural relations (e.g., graph pruning \[[377](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib377), [431](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib431)\]). During the generation phase, squeezing interdependency (e.g., positional encoding \[[361](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib361), [549](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib549)\]) to the generator would encode richer structural signals into the generated content.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Difference 3 - Domain Invariance vs. Domain-specific Information</span>: The relations in graph-structured data are domain-specific. Unlike images and texts, where different domains often share transferable semantics \[[254](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib254), [286](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib286)\], such as textures and grains in images or vocabulary defined by the tokenizer in texts, graph-structured data lacks explicit transferable units. This shared basis in images and texts lays the foundation for designing encoders with geometric invariance and enables the well-known data-scaling law. However, for graph-structured data, the underlying data generation process governing the generated graphs varies significantly across different domains. This variability makes the relational information highly domain-specific, and it is nearly impossible to design a unified GraphRAG applicable to different domains. For example, when predicting the topic of an academic paper, the widely accepted homophily assumption suggests retrieving references from the paper to inform its topic prediction \[[563](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib563)\]. However, this homophily assumption is not suitable when classifying the role of an airport in a flight network, where hubs are often sparsely distributed across a country with no direct connections \[[68](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib68)\]. Moreover, even within the same graph from the same domain, different tasks may necessitate distinct GraphRAG designs. For example, when designing an automatic email completion system to optimize communication efficiency in a company, both content relevance and tone coherence should be considered \[[429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\]. To ensure the content relevance of the generated emails, one might assume that close emails (i.e., emails from the same conversation thread) share similar content and thus should be retrieved for reference. However, to maintain tone coherence, emails from staff with similar roles might be retrieved, even if they do not share close social relations (e.g., between subordinates and superiors) but instead hold similar structural roles within the company (e.g., as managers of different teams).

![Refer to caption](extracted/6113564/Figure/rag-grag.png)

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 1: </span> RAG works on text and images, which can be uniformly formatted as 1D sequences or 2D grids with no relational information. In contrast, GraphRAG works on graph-structured data, which encompasses diverse formats and includes domain-specific relational information.

Despite the above differences that have driven extensive research in GraphRAG, the current research landscape in this field remains fragmented, with significant variation in concepts, techniques, and datasets across studies. Moreover, current GraphRAG research primarily focuses on knowledge and document graphs as surveyed in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S1.F2 "Figure 2 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)"), often overlooking broader applications in other domains like infrastructure graphs. This imbalance not only hampers the advancement of GraphRAG but also risks creating a "bubble effect" that restricts the scope of future exploration. To address these challenges, we present a comprehensive and up-to-date review of GraphRAG, aiming to unify the GraphRAG framework from the global perspective while also specializing its unique design for each domain from the local perspective. The key contributions of this survey are as follows:

![Refer to caption](extracted/6113564/Figure/count_paper_annotated.png)

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 2: </span> Publications of GraphRAGs in different domains based on surveyed papers

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.I2.i1.p1.1.1">A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG</span>: We propose a holistic framework of GraphRAG consisting of five key components: query processor, retriever, organizer, generator, and graph data source. Within each component, we review representative GraphRAG techniques.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.I2.i2.p1.10.1">Specialization of GraphRAG in different domains</span>: We categorize GraphRAG designs into 10 distinct domains based on their specific applications, including knowledge graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_kg.png), document graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_doc.png), scientific graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_sci.png), social graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_soc.png), planning & reasoning graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_plan.png), tabular graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_tab.png), infrastructure graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_infra.png), biological graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_bio.png), scene graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_scene.png), and random graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_random.png). For each domain, we review their unique applications and specific graph construction methods. We then summarize the distinctive designs of each component within our proposed holistic GraphRAG framework and collect rich benchmark datasets and tool resources.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S1.I2.i3.p1.1.1">Challenges and Future Directions:</span> We highlight the challenges of current GraphRAG research and pinpoint future opportunities for further advancing GraphRAG into the new frontier.

In the following, we highlight the differences between our survey and existing surveys. Despite the urgent need for a systematic overview of GraphRAG, most existing surveys focus on general RAG within the context of i.i.d. data \[[11](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib11), [120](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib120), [227](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib227), [551](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib551), [561](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib561)\]. Before the advent of LLMs, earlier surveys focused on textual RAGs \[[11](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib11), [227](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib227)\]. With the recent unprecedented success achieved by foundational models such as LLMs, various surveys have explored foundational-model-powered RAG in different modalities. Gao et al. \[[120](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib120)\] group existing RAG approaches into three categories (Naive, Advanced, and Modular RAGs), summarize three core techniques (Retrieval, Generation, and Augmentation), and review evaluation metrics. In parallel, Zhao et al. \[[551](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib551)\] review representative RAG systems according to their corresponding application and data modality. \[[561](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib561)\] focuses on reviewing trustworthy concerns and techniques of RAG. However, none of them have a dedicated focus on graph-structured data. To the best of our knowledge, only one very recent study \[[319](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib319)\] has specifically surveyed RAG in the context of graph-structured data. However, this work mainly focuses on reviewing techniques introduced by graphs under the conventional RAG architecture without specializing in reviewing diverse relations and technical designs for graphs across different domains. In contrast to its holistic review philosophy, we recognize the inherent heterogeneity of graph-structured data and specialize our GraphRAG review across different domains. Specifically, we uncover the fundamental task applications (when to retrieve), graph construction methods and relational rationales (what to retrieve), and GraphRAG techniques (how to retrieve) for each domain. In this way, our survey provides a comprehensive overview of GraphRAG for information retrieval, data mining, and machine learning communities and domain-specific insights that facilitate interdisciplinary research and industrial opportunities.

Our survey is structured as follows: Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2 "2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)") introduces the holistic framework of GraphRAG and introduces representative techniques for its five key components. From Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S3 "3 Knowledge Graph ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)") to [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">9</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S9 "9 Other Domains ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)"), we delve into specific domains, reviewing unique task applications, summarizing existing graph construction methods that guide GraphRAG design for that domain, highlighting domain-specific techniques for each of the five components within our proposed holistic framework, and presenting existing GraphRAG resources (e.g., benchmark datasets and tools) used across different domains. Finally, we discuss research challenges and opportunities in Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">10</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S10 "10 Challenges and Future Work ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)") and conclude our survey in Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">11</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S11 "11 Conclusion ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)").

![Refer to caption](extracted/6113564/Figure/framework.png)

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 3: </span> A holistic framework of GraphRAG and representative techniques for its key components.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">2 </span> A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG

Based on existing literature on GraphRAG, we present a holistic framework of GraphRAG. Next, we introduce the basic problem setting and notation used throughout the whole framework.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.1 </span> Problem Setting and Notations

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

id="S2.I1.i5.p1.1.m1.2d">italic_A = bold_Ω start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT Generator end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( over^ start_ARG italic_Q end_ARG , over^ start_ARG italic_C end_ARG )</annotation></semantics></math> to answer query <math alttext="Q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">Q</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝑄</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1c">Q</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I1.i5.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_Q</annotation></semantics></math>.

Unlike sequential-based textual data and grid-structured image data, graph-structured data encapsulates relational information. To effectively harness this relational information, the above five core components of GraphRAG desire dedicated designs to handle graph-structured input/output and support graph-based operations. For example, in the retriever component, conventional RAG in the Natural Language Processing (NLP) utilizes sparse/dense encoders for index search \[[196](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib196), [468](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib468)\]. In contrast, GraphRAG employs graph traversal methods (e.g., entity linking and BFS/DFS) and graph-based encoders (e.g., Graph Neural Networks (GNNs)) to produce embeddings for retrieval. This motivates us to summarize key innovations and representative designs of GraphRAG for each of the above five components under the holistic GraphRAG framework in the following.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.2 </span> Task Applications and Example Query <math alttext="Q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1b"><mi id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.1.m1.1.1.cmml">Q</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1c"><ci id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.1.m1.1.1">𝑄</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1d">Q</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.1.m1.1e">italic_Q</annotation></semantics></math>

Similar to the general RAG framework where the text-formatted query <math alttext="Q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">Q</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑄</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1c">Q</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_Q</annotation></semantics></math> specifies the question context or the task instruction. Query <math alttext="Q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">Q</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝑄</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1c">Q</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_Q</annotation></semantics></math> in GraphRAG could also be in the format of text. For example, in knowledge graph-based question-answering, the query could be "What is the Capital of China?" \[[272](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib272), [395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395)\]. In addition, the query could also be in other formats, such as smile strings for molecular graphs \[[132](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib132)\], or could even be the combination of multiple formats, such as the scene graph along with the text instruction \[[147](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib147)\]. Table [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.T1 "Table 1 ‣ 2.2 Task Applications and Example Query 𝑄 ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)") summarizes the common task applications and exemplary queries used in each domain, as well as their representative references.

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 1: </span> Summary of Task Applications and Exemplary Queries for GraphRAG in each domain.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.3 </span> Query Processor <math alttext="\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\text{Processor}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1b"><msup id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">𝛀</mi><mtext id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml">Processor</mtext></msup><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1c"><apply id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1">superscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.2">𝛀</ci><ci id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.3"><mtext id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S2.SS3.1.m1.1.1.3">Processor</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1d">\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\text{Processor}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.1.m1.1e">bold_Ω start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT Processor end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>

Unlike RAG, where both queries and data sources are purely text-formatted, data sources used in GraphRAG are graph-structured, which raises challenges in bridging text-formatted queries and graph-structured data sources. For example, the information that connects the knowledge graph and the query "Who is Justin Bieber’s brother?" is not a specific passage but instead the entity "Justin Bieber" and the relation "brother of". Many techniques are proposed to correctly extract this information from the query, including entity recognition, relational extraction, query structuration, query decomposition, and query expansion. In the following, we first review each of these five query processing techniques within the broader NLP domain, followed by a focused examination of their unique adaptations for GraphRAG.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.3.1 </span> Name Entity Recognition

Named Entity Recognition (NER) aims to identify mentions of entities from the text that belong to predefined categories, such as persons, locations, or organizations, and it serves as a fundamental component for numerous natural language applications \[[160](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib160), [199](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib199), [228](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib228), [293](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib293)\]. NER techniques can be broadly categorized into four main approaches: (1) rule-based methods, which rely entirely on handcrafted rules and require no annotated data; (2) unsupervised learning methods, which use unsupervised algorithms without labeled training examples; (3) feature-based supervised learning methods, which depend on supervised algorithms and careful feature engineering; and (4) deep learning approaches, which automatically discover representations needed after (un)-supervised training the deep learning models. Recent LLMs fall into the category of deep learning approaches and have demonstrated unprecedented success for NER. More details about these techniques and their resources can be found in Li et al. \[[228](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib228)\].

Specifically, in the GraphRAG context, entity recognition primarily uses deep learning techniques (e.g., EntityLinker \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\] and LLM-based extraction \[[186](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib186)\]) to identify entities in queries grounded by nodes in the given graph data sources. This step is vital for applications such as knowledge graph-based question answering \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\]. For example, given the question, "What is the best way to guess the color of the eye of the baby?", NER extracts entities such as "baby", "eye", and "color", which correspond to nodes in the knowledge graph and are treated as the seed nodes to initialize the retrieval process thereafter \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443), [347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347)\]. For more recent GraphRAG research, NER has evolved beyond identifying the entity names but instead their structures. For example, Jin et al. \[[186](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib186)\] leverages LLMs to recognize node types in the graph, which further guides the retriever to identify nodes that match the recognized types for next-round exploration. For example, given the question "Who are the authors of ‘Language Models are Unsupervised Multi-task Learners’?" the initially recognized entity should not only be based on the semantic name "Language Models are Unsupervised Multi-task Learners" but also be based on the type of that entity, which is the paper node in this case. Accurately recognizing the names and structures of entities in GraphRAG reduces cascading errors and provides a solid foundation for subsequent retrieval and generation steps.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.3.2 </span> Relational Extraction

Similar to NER, relational extraction (RE) is a long-standing technique in NLP to identify relations among entities and is widely applied to structured search, sentiment analysis, question answering, summarization, and knowledge graph construction \[[47](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib47), [230](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib230), [303](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib303)\]. Recent advances in RE have been largely driven by deep learning techniques, and they can be summarized into three perspectives: text representation, context encoding, and triplet prediction, more details of which can be found in Pawar et al. \[[317](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib317)\], Nasar et al. \[[303](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib303)\], Han et al. \[[141](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib141)\].

For GraphRAG, RE serves two key purposes: constructing graph-structured data sources (e.g., knowledge graphs) by extracting triplets and matching the relations mentioned in the query and the graph data source to guide the graph search. For instance, given a query like "What is the capital of China?", relational extraction identifies the relation "capital of" and searches for corresponding edges via vector similarity in the knowledge graph, which guides the neighborhood selection and graph traversal direction \[[119](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib119), [200](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib200), [272](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib272), [273](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib273)\].

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.3.3 </span> Query Structuration

Query structuration transforms queries into formats tailored to specific data sources and tasks. It often converts natural language queries into structured formats like SQL or SPARQL \[[181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181), [238](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib238)\] to interact with relational databases. Recent advancements leverage pre-trained and fine-tuned LLMs to generate structured queries from natural language input to query databases. For graph-structured data, Graph Query Language (GQL) has emerged, such as Cypher, GraphQL, and SPARQL, which enables complex interactions with property graph databases. Additionally, Jin et al. \[[186](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib186)\] introduced a technique that decomposes complex queries into multiple structured operations, including node retrieval, feature fetching, neighbor checks, and degree assessment, enhancing precision and adaptability in querying.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.3.4 </span> Query Decomposition

Query decomposition \[[447](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib447)\] aims to split the input query into multiple distinct subqueries, which are used to first retrieve sub-results and aggregate these sub-results together for the final results. In most existing RAG and GraphRAG, decomposed queries usually possess explicit logic connections that can handle complex tasks that require multistep reasoning and planning \[[248](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib248), [316](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib316), [355](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib355), [372](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib372), [477](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib477)\]. For example, a query like "Please generate an image where a girl is reading a book, and her pose is the same as the boy in ‘example.jpg’ then describe the new image with your voice" involves multiple subtasks \[[477](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib477)\], each of which would be completed by a specific sub-query. In addition, Park et al. \[[316](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib316)\] enhance the decomposition of the query by building a question graph where each sub-query is represented as a triplet within the graph. These graph-structured sub-queries effectively guide the retriever/generator through multi-step promptings.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.3.5 </span> Query Expansion

Query Expansion enriches a query by adding meaningful terms with similar significance \[[12](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib12)\], which primarily addresses three challenges: (1) user-submitted queries are ambiguous and relate to multiple topics; (2) queries may be too brief to fully capture user intent; and (3) users are often uncertain about what they are seeking. Generally, it can be categorized into manual query expansion, automatic query expansion, and interactive query expansion. More recently, LLM-based query expansion has been a prominent area due to the creativity of the generated content\[[54](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib54), [173](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib173), [221](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib221)\]

Unlike existing methods that mostly focus on textual similarities and overlook relations, QE in GraphRAG augments LLM expansion with structured relations. For example Xia et al. \[[459](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib459)\] expands the query by leveraging neighboring nodes of the mentioned entities in the query. Alternatively, Wang et al. \[[406](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib406)\] convert the query into several sub-queries using pre-defined templates.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.4 </span> Retriever <math alttext="\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\textbf{Retriever}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1b"><msup id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">𝛀</mi><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_bold" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml">Retriever</mtext></msup><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1c"><apply id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1">superscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.2">𝛀</ci><ci id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.3"><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_bold" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S2.SS4.1.m1.1.1.3">Retriever</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1d">\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\textbf{Retriever}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.1.m1.1e">bold_Ω start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT Retriever end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>

end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( over^ start_ARG italic_Q end_ARG , italic_G )</annotation></semantics></math>

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(1)</span>

Recently, retrievers have been increasingly integrated with LLMs to mitigate hallucination issues \[[397](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib397)\], address privacy concerns \[[513](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib513)\], and enhance explainability and dynamic adaptability \[[360](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib360), [419](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib419)\]. While effective, they are predominantly designed for texts and images and not readily transferable to graph-structured data for GraphRAG for two reasons. First, the input/output format of GraphRAG differs significantly from that of traditional RAG. While most retrievers in RAG use NLP tokenizers for encoders and adhere to the "Text-in, Text-out" workflow, the workflow of GraphRAG is more diverse, including "Text-in, Text-out" \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\], "Text-in, Graph-out" \[[454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454), [569](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib569)\], "Graph-in, Text-out" and "Graph-in, Graph-out" processes \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433)\]. Secondly, retrievers in traditional RAGs do not capture graph structure signals. Methods like BM25 and TF-IDF \[[337](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib337), [333](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib333)\] primarily focus on lexical signals, and deep-learning-based retrievers \[[196](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib196)\] usually capture semantic signals, both of which overlook the graph structure signals. This motivates us to review existing GraphRAG retrievers, i.e., heuristic-based, learning-based, and domain-specific retrievers, with a particular emphasis on their unique technical design adapted to graph-structured data.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.4.1 </span> Heuristic-based Retriever

Heuristic-based retrievers primarily use predefined rules, domain-specific insights, and hard-coded algorithms to extract relevant information from graph data sources. Their reliance on explicit rules often makes them more time/resource-efficient compared to deep learning models. For instance, simple graph traversal methods like BFS or DFS can be executed in linear time without needing training data. However, this same reliance on fixed heuristics also limits their adaptability to generalize to unseen scenarios. In the following, we review the heuristic-based retrievers commonly used in GraphRAG.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p2.1.1">Entity Linking</span>: In heuristic-based retrievers, entity linking involves mapping entities identified in the query to corresponding nodes in graph data sources. This mapping forms an initial bridge between the query and the graph, serving as either the retriever by itself or as a foundation for further graph traversal to broaden the scope of the retrieval. The effectiveness of this approach relies on accurate entity recognition conducted by the query processor and the quality of labeled entities on graph nodes. This technique is commonly applied in knowledge graphs, where Top-K nodes are selected as starting points based on their textual similarity to the query. The similarity metric can be computed using vector embeddings \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443), [347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347)\] and lexical features \[[428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\]. More recently, LLMs have been used as knowledgeable context augmenters to generate mention-centered descriptions as additional input to augment the long-tail entities where their limited training data usually cause the entity linking model to struggle to disambiguate \[[464](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib464)\].

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p3.1.1">Relational Matching</span>: Relational matching, similar to entity linking, is a heuristic-based retrieval approach designed to identify edges within graph data sources that align with the relations specified in a query. This method is crucial for tasks that focus on identifying relationships among entities in a graph. The matched edges guide the traversal process by indicating which edges to explore next based on the entities and relations encountered in the graph data sources. Similar to entity linking, Top-K edges are selected based on their similarity to each edge in the graph \[[200](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib200), [119](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib119)\].

In addition to the efficiency and simplicity of the above two types of heuristic-based retrievers, another key advantage is their ability to overcome ambiguity. For example, although machine/deep learning-based retrievers are difficult to differentiate semantically/lexically similar entities/relations (e.g., Byte vs. Bit, and President of vs. Resident of), these heuristic methods can easily distinguish them based on pre-defined rules, even in cases where semantic/lexical differences are subtle.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.1">Graph Traversal</span>: After performing entity linking and relational matching to identify initial nodes and relations in graph data sources, graph traversal algorithms (e.g., BFS, DFS) can expand this set to uncover additional query-relevant information. However, a core challenge for traversal-based retrieval is the risk of information overload, as the exponentially expanding neighborhood often includes substantial irrelevant content. To address this, current traversal techniques integrate adaptive retrieval and filtering processes, selectively exploring the most relevant neighboring nodes and incrementally refining the retrieved content to minimize noise. This graph traversal is mainly used in GraphRAG for knowledge and document graphs. When traversing on these two types of graphs, many methods extract all paths less than length <math alttext="l" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml">l</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1.1">𝑙</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1c">l</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.1.m1.1d">italic_l</annotation></semantics></math> <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.2">between</span> the nodes identified by entity linking \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493), [530](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib530), [185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185), [110](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib110)\], while others consider the <math alttext="l" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml">l</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1.1">𝑙</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1c">l</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p5.2.m2.1d">italic_l</annotation></semantics></math>\-hop subgraph around the initial entities \[[308](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib308), [395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181), [205](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib205)\]. To more efficiently traverse the KG, other methods prune irrelevant paths via the use of a LLM \[[271](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib271), [347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428), [134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134)\] and others use pre-defined rules or templates to traverse the graph \[[406](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib406), [246](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib246), [72](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib72)\].

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p6.1.1">Graph Kernel</span>: Compared with the above heuristic-based retrievers for retrieving nodes, edges, and their combined subgraphs, some earlier works (e.g., graph extraction and image retrieval) \[[448](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib448), [218](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib218), [123](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib123)\] treat the text and image as the entire graph and use graph-level heuristics such as graph kernels to measure similarity and retrieve. Graph kernels measure pairwise similarities by calculating inner products between graphs, aligning both structural and semantic aspects of the query and the retrieved graphs. Notable examples include the random-walk kernel and the Weisfeiler Leman kernel \[[357](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib357), [403](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib403)\]. The random walk kernel computes similarity by performing simultaneous random walks on two graphs and counting the number of matching paths. The Weisfeiler Leman kernel iteratively applies the Weisfeiler Leman algorithm to produce color distributions of node labels at each iteration and then calculates similarity based on the inner products of these histogram vectors. For example, Wu et al. \[[448](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib448)\] constructs event graphs of both documents and queries and uses a product graph kernel that counts walks between two graphs to measure the query-document similarity and rank the documents. Lebrun et al. \[[218](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib218)\] conducts event graph matching by introducing a fast and efficient graph-matching kernel for image retrieval. Similarly, Glavaš and Šnajder \[[123](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib123)\] translates images into representative attribute structural graphs that capture spatial relations among regions and perform graph kernel based on random walks to derive hash codes for image retrieval.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS1.p7.1.1">Domain Expertise</span>: The domain-agnostic nature of traditional heuristic-based methods restricts their effectiveness in areas that require specialized expertise. For instance, in drug discovery, chemists typically design drugs by referencing existing molecules with desirable properties rather than constructing molecular structures from scratch. These molecules are selected based on domain knowledge that guides the retrieval of structures with similar characteristics. Following this intuition, many GraphRAG systems incorporate domain expertise to enhance retriever design. Wang et al. \[[434](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib434)\] develop a hybrid retrieval system that integrates heuristic-based and learning-based retrieval to retrieve exemplar molecules that partially meet the target design criteria.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.4.2 </span> Learning-based Retriever

One significant limitation of heuristic-based retrievers is their over-reliance on pre-defined rules, which limits their generalizability to data that does not strictly adhere to these rules. For example, when confronted with entities that have slight semantic or structural variations, such as "doctor" and "physician", heuristic-based retrievers like entity linking may treat them differently due to their distinct lexical representations, despite their shared underlying meaning. To overcome this limitation, learning-based retrievers have been proposed to capture deeper, more abstract, and task-relevant relations between the query and objects in data sources, which avoid relying solely on hard-coded rules. These retrievers often work by uniformly compressing information of various formats (e.g., texts and images) into embeddings based on machine learning encoders and then fetching relevant information by conducting an embedding-based similarity search. Notably, some entity linking and relational matching methods that employ machine learning encoders to generate embeddings for matching should also be considered as learning-based retrievers.

In conventional RAG, assuming the query <math alttext="q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p2.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p2.1.m1.1.1" <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(2)</span>

Unlike RAGs that use language and vision encoders to embed texts and images, encoders used in GraphRAG retrieval extend beyond independently and identically distributed (i.i.d.) data by embedding nodes, edges, and (sub)graphs. Depending on the input format, the encoder could be a text encoder for query, a graph-based encoder for graph structure, and an integrated text-and-graph encoder for the textual attributed graph \[[55](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib55), [56](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib56)\]. We specifically focus on graph-based encoders. Existing graph-based encoders can be broadly categorized into shallow embedding methods – such as Node2Vec and DeepWalk – and deep embedding methods like Graph Neural Networks (GNNs). Below, we review these two encoders and their unique roles in GraphRAG.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p4.1.1">Shallow Embedding Methods</span>: Shallow embedding methods \[[114](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib114)\], like Node2Vec \[[127](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib127)\] and Role2Vec \[[5](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib5)\], learn node, edge, and graph embeddings that retain the essential structural information of the original graph. Based on the type of structural information that can be extracted, these methods generally fall into two categories: proximity/role-based embeddings. Proximity-based methods, such as DeepWalk and Node2Vec \[[127](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib127), [321](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib321)\], focus on preserving the proximity of connected nodes, ensuring that nodes close in the graph also remain close in the embedding space. Role-based methods, like Role2Vec and GraphWave \[[5](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib5), [93](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib93)\], generate node embeddings based on their structural roles rather than their proximity relations. In general, these methods initialize each node with a latent embedding vector and conduct unsupervised training to squeeze structural signals derived from graph structure into the embedding. In GraphRAG, proximity-based shallow embeddings can effectively retrieve entities that are proximally close, while role-based embeddings can capture entities that share similar roles. For instance, proximity-based embeddings could be used to retrieve academic papers by fetching papers sharing similar research topics or retrieve reviews from products that are co-purchased with the current product \[[346](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib346), [429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\]. Meanwhile, role-based embeddings could support tasks like generating company emails by retrieving similar emails based on shared roles or tones \[[429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\].

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.1">Deep Embedding Methods</span>: Although shallow embedding methods incorporate structural signals into learned embeddings for nodes, edges, or entire graphs, they struggle to leverage semantic features—like bag-of-words representations for academic paper retrieval or atomic numbers for molecular retrieval \[[114](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib114)\]. Additionally, these methods lack inductivity, requiring re-initialization and retraining whenever new nodes, edges, or graphs are added. This limitation significantly reduces their applicability in GraphRAG retrieval tasks as real-world knowledge evolves dynamically where new information continually replaces outdated content, such as in citation networks, social graphs, and knowledge graphs \[[59](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib59), [419](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib419), [453](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib453)\]. To address these limitations, deep embedding methods have been proposed, which not only jointly fuse features and graph structures to obtain embeddings for retrieval but also inherent inductive property as the newly coming nodes/edges/graphs share common feature space with the ones during the training phase. One of the most representative and powerful approaches in this category is GNN, which combines the power of message-passing to encode structural signals and feature transformation to extract task-relevant information. Mathematically, <math alttext="l^{\text{th}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1a"><msup id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">l</mi><mtext id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml">th</mtext></msup><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1">superscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑙</ci><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3"><mtext id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3">th</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1c">l^{\text{th}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p5.1.m1.1d">italic_l start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT th end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>\-layer graph convolution can be formulated as:

<math alttext="\displaystyle\mathbf{x}_{i}^{l}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.E3.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.E3.m1.1a"><msubsup id="S2.E3.m1.1.1" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.2" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.2.cmml">𝐱</mi><mi id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.3" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.3.cmml">i</mi><mi id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.3.cmml">l</mi></msubsup><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.E3.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1">superscript</csymbol><apply id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.2.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.2">𝐱</ci><ci id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.3.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.2.3">𝑖</ci></apply><ci id="S2.E3.m1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.E3.m1.1.1.3">𝑙</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.E3.m1.1c">\displaystyle\mathbf{x}_{i}^{l}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.E3.m1.1d">bold_x start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_l end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>

<math alttext="\displaystyle=\gamma_{\boldsymbol{\Theta}_{\gamma}}\left(\mathbf{x}_{i}^{l-1}%

xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.8.m8.1.1.3.2">𝑖</ci><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.8.m8.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.8.m8.1.1.3.3">𝑗</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.8.m8.1c">\mathcal{N}^{e}_{ij}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.8.m8.1d">caligraphic_N start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_e end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i italic_j end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>, as shown by Eq ([<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.E4 "In 2.4.2 Learning-based Retriever ‣ 2.4 Retriever 𝛀^\"Retriever\" ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)")). Graph-level embeddings could be obtained by further applying pooling operation <math alttext="\rho_{\boldsymbol{\Theta}_{\rho}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1a"><msub id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.2.cmml">ρ</mi><msub id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.2" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.2.cmml">𝚯</mi><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.3" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.3.cmml">ρ</mi></msub></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1b"><apply id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.2">𝜌</ci><apply id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.2">𝚯</ci><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1.1.3.3">𝜌</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1c">\rho_{\boldsymbol{\Theta}_{\rho}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.9.m9.1d">italic_ρ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT bold_Θ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_ρ end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> over node and edge embeddings, as shown by Eq ([<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.E5 "In 2.4.2 Learning-based Retriever ‣ 2.4 Retriever 𝛀^\"Retriever\" ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)")). Following this GNN-based embedding paradigm, various forms of graph knowledge from diverse sources—such as nodes, edges, and (sub)graphs—can be uniformly embedded into vector representations, as shown in Figure [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.F5 "Figure 5 ‣ 2.4 Retriever 𝛀^\"Retriever\" ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)")(c) where we derive embeddings for nodes (<math alttext="\mathbf{X}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1.1.cmml">𝐗</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1b"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1.1">𝐗</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1c">\mathbf{X}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.10.m10.1d">bold_X</annotation></semantics></math>), edges (<math alttext="\mathbf{E}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1.1.cmml">𝐄</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1b"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1.1">𝐄</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1c">\mathbf{E}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.11.m11.1d">bold_E</annotation></semantics></math>), and graphs (<math alttext="\mathbf{G}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1.1.cmml">𝐆</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1b"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1.1">𝐆</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1c">\mathbf{G}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p7.12.m12.1d">bold_G</annotation></semantics></math>).

Having obtained these node/edge/graph-level embeddings further enables us to create embeddings for different types of structures (<math alttext="\mathbf{S}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1.1.cmml">𝐒</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1.1">𝐒</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1c">\mathbf{S}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.1.m1.1d">bold_S</annotation></semantics></math>) by combining these sub-structure embeddings according to specific configurations for each structure. For instance, if the retrieved subgraph is a path within a knowledge graph, we can aggregate the embeddings of the nodes and relations along that path to form a cohesive path embedding.<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote1"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">1</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">1</span>Incorporating structural signals may be necessary, a consideration to be addressed in future work.</span></span></span> Eventually, the resulting embeddings for different structures can be utilized either during the training phase to optimize query alignment or during the testing phase to enable similarity-based neural search. For example, GNN-RAG \[[347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347)\] uses a GNN to perform retrieval, where a separate round of message passing is performed for each query. The query <math alttext="\hat{Q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1a"><mover accent="true" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">Q</mi><mo id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml">^</mo></mover><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1"><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.1">^</ci><ci id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1.1.2">𝑄</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1c">\hat{Q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS4.SSS2.p8.2.m2.1d">over^ start_ARG italic_Q end_ARG</annotation></semantics></math> is incorporated into the message passing by, including its embedding in the message computation. A set of “candidate” nodes is chosen which have a probability of being relevant greater than some threshold. The shortest path from the query nodes to each candidate node is retrieved as context. Liu et al. \[[251](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib251)\] consider the use of a conditional GNN \[[163](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib163)\] where only the linked entities from the query are initialized to a non-zero representation. The candidate nodes are chosen in a similar manner to \[[347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347)\]. A single path is then retrieved for each candidate node and is extracted by backtracking until we reach a query node. REANO \[[106](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib106)\] encodes the query information into an edge-specific attention weight, conditional on the query. After aggregation, the top k triples most similar to the query are chosen as context.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.4.3 </span> Advanced Retrieval Strategies

Real-world queries are often complex and encode multi-aspect intentions, possess structure patterns, and desire multi-hop reasoning that the aforementioned basic retrievers struggle to address. For example, answering "What is the name of the fight song of the university whose main campus is in Lawrence, Kansas, and whose branch campuses are in the Kansas City metropolitan area?" demands multi-hop reasoning to identify the university based on location and retrieve information about its fight song \[[145](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib145), [399](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib399)\]. Similarly, a query like "What are the main themes in the dataset?" requires understanding the product community structure, retrieving themes for each community, and aggregating the identified themes together to summarize the main theme \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\]. Furthermore, when asking "Who is the most impactful research scholar in deep learning?" the answer could vary depending on multiple aspects \[[536](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib536)\], such as the number of citations, the volume of published papers, or the number of co-authors. Accurately addressing such queries requires a deeper understanding of the underlying data distribution to discern which aspect the query prioritizes. To address these highly complex queries, advanced retrieval strategies have been proposed, and we review them as follows:

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS3.p2.1.1">Integrated Retrieval:</span> Integrated retrieval combines various types of retrievers to capture relevant information by balancing their strengths and weaknesses. Typically, integrated retrieval approaches are categorized according to which individual retrievers are used in combination, with notable examples including neural-symbolic retrieval \[[83](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib83), [220](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib220), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\] and multimodal retrieval \[[69](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib69), [266](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib266)\].

Since the knowledge stored in graph-structured data exists mostly in symbolic format, neural-symbolic retrieval is a natural choice for the integrated retrieval strategy in GraphRAG. This strategy interleaves rule-based patterns for retrieving symbolic knowledge with neural-based signals for retrieving more abstract and deep knowledge. For example, Luo et al. \[[272](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib272)\], Wen et al. \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443)\] first expands the neighbors based on the knowledge of the symbolic knowledge graph and then performs path retrieval using neural matching. In contrast, Mavromatis and Karypis \[[289](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib289)\] first utilizes GNNs to retrieve seed entities (neural retrieval) and then extract the shortest paths from seed entities (symbolic retrieval). Similarly, Tian et al. \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395)\], Yasunaga et al. \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\], Wang et al. \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\], Luo et al. \[[272](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib272)\] fetch the k-hop neighborhood of the entities mentioned in the current question-answering pair and the session of user-generated items as the answer candidates (symbolic retrieval) and compute attention between the query and the extracted subgraph to differentiate candidate relevance (neural retrieval).

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS3.p4.1.1">Iterative Retrieval:</span> Iterative retrieval is a multistep process where consecutive retrieval operations share common dependencies such as causal, resource, and temporal dependency. These dependencies can be implicitly characterized by the retrieval order in RAG \[[399](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib399), [481](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib481)\] or explicitly modeled as a graph structure in GraphRAG \[[145](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib145), [454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454)\]. Consequently, iterative retrieval is primarily utilized in GraphRAG to capture these dependencies. For example, KGP \[[419](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib419)\] alternates between generating the next piece of evidence for the question and selecting the most promising neighbor. ToG \[[381](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib381)\] starts by identifying initial entities and then iteratively expands reasoning paths until enough information is gathered to answer the question. StructGPT \[[181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181)\] pre-defines graph interfaces and prompts LLMs to iteratively invoke these interfaces until sufficient information is collected.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.SS4.SSS3.p5.1.1">Adaptive Retrieval:</span> While retrieved external knowledge offers benefits, it also introduces risks. If the generator already possesses sufficient internal knowledge for a task, the retrieved external information may be unnecessary or even conflicting \[[42](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib42), [473](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib473)\]. Specifically, when internal knowledge fully covers the necessary information, retrieval becomes redundant and may introduce contradictions. To mitigate this, knowledge checking has been proposed in RAG systems \[[176](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib176), [189](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib189), [412](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib412), [490](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib490)\]. This approach allows the system to adaptively assess when and how much external information is needed. By equipping the retriever with this adaptability, RAG can provide more intelligent, flexible, and context-aware responses, fostering better harmony between internal and external knowledge sources.

One of the adaptive retrievals in GraphRAG is designed by considering different reasoning depths for different queries, i.e., too few hops of graph traversal might overlook critical reasoning relations, while too many can introduce unnecessary noise. Guo et al. \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134)\], Wu et al. \[[455](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib455)\] address this by training models to predict the required number of hops for a given query and retrieving the relevant graph content accordingly. No existing works focus on resolving knowledge conflicts in GraphRAG, and therefore, we leave this discussion to future work.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.5 </span> Organizer

After retrieving the relevant content <math alttext="C" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS5.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS5.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS5.p1.1.m1.1.1" sources, which may be in the format of entities, relations, triplets, paths or subgraphs, the organizer <math alttext="\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\textbf{Organizer}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline"
