end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> processes this content in conjunction with the processed query <math alttext="\hat{Q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1a"><mover accent="true" id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml">Q</mi><mo id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml">^</mo></mover><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1b"><apply id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1"><ci id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.1">^</ci><ci id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1.1.2">𝑄</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1c">\hat{Q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS5.p1.3.m3.1d">over^ start_ARG italic_Q end_ARG</annotation></semantics></math>. The aim is to post-process and refine the retrieved content to better adapt it for generator consumption, thereby further improving the quality of the downstream content generation. Formally, the organizer is represented as follows:

<math alttext="\displaystyle\hat{C}=\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\textbf{Organizer}}(\hat{Q},C)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.E6.m1.2"><semantics id="S2.E6.m1.2a"><mrow id="S2.E6.m1.2.3" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.cmml"><mover accent="true" id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.2" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.2.cmml"><mi id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.2.2" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.2.2.cmml">C</mi><mo id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.2.1" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.2.1.cmml">^</mo></mover><mo id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.1" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.1.cmml">=</mo><mrow id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3.cmml"><msup id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3.2" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3.2.cmml"><mi id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3.2.2" xref="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3.2.2.cmml">𝛀</mi><mtext class="ltx_mathvariant_bold" id="S2.E6.m1.2.3.3.2.3" id="S2.E6.m1.2c">\displaystyle\hat{C}=\boldsymbol{\Omega}^{\textbf{Organizer}}(\hat{Q},C)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.E6.m1.2d">over^ start_ARG italic_C end_ARG = bold_Ω start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT Organizer end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT ( over^ start_ARG italic_Q end_ARG , italic_C )</annotation></semantics></math>

<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(6)</span>

In GraphRAG, the need for fine-grained organization and refinement of retrieved content is driven by several key reasons. Firstly, when the retrieved contents are subgraphs, their heterogeneous format of knowledge in terms of node/edge features and graph structures becomes more likely to include irrelevant and noisy information, which poses significant difficulty for LLM to digest and thus compromises the generation quality. This raises the desire for graph pruning techniques to polish the retrieved subgraph and remove task-irrelevant knowledge. Secondly, LLMs have been widely demonstrated to possess attention biases toward certain positions of relevant information within the retrieved context \[[43](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib43)\]. Therefore, the exponentially growing neighbors as the receptive field enlarges (i.e., the number of hops increases) in the retrieved subgraphs would also exponentially increase the amount of context length in the prompt and dilute the focus of LLMs on the task-relevant knowledge \[[358](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib358)\]. This poses a new requirement for the graph-based reranking mechanism to prioritize the most important content within the retrieved graph. Thirdly, the retrieved content might be incomplete in terms of both the semantic content and the structural content, which necessitates graph augmentation for the enhancement. Finally, the retrieved content is often a graph, which not only possesses semantic content information but also owns its unique structure. This complex structural content is not easily consumed by LLMs that are trained by next-token prediction coupled with linearized prompting, which requires structure-aware verbalization techniques to reorganize. We will formally review each of the above-motivated organizer techniques in the following sections.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.5.1 </span> Graph Pruning

In GraphRAG, the retrieved graph can be large and potentially contain a significant amount of noisy and redundant information. For example, when graph traversal methods are applied in retrieval, the size of the retrieved subgraph exponentially increases with the number of hops. Large subgraph sizes not only increase computational costs but can also reduce generation quality due to the inclusion of noisy information. In contrast, if the number of hops is too small, the retrieved subgraph may be too small to include crucial knowledge required by tasks. To achieve a better trade-off between the size of the retrieved subgraph and the amount of its encoded task-relevant information, various graph pruning methods have been proposed to reduce the size of subgraphs by removing irrelevant nodes and edges while preserving the essential information.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I2.i1.p1.1.1">Semantic-based pruning:</span> Semantic-based pruning focuses on reducing the graph size by removing nodes and edge relations that are semantically irrelevant to the query. For example, QA-GNN \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492)\] prunes irrelevant nodes with low relevance scores by encoding the query context and node labels using LLMs, followed by a linear projection. GraphQA \[[389](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib389)\] further removes clusters of nodes with the lowest relevance to the query. KnowledgeNavigator \[[133](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib133)\] scores the relations in the retrieved graph based on the query and prunes irrelevant relations to reduce graph size. Additionally, Gao et al. \[[118](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib118)\] partition the retrieved subgraph into smaller subgraphs and then ranks them with only the top-k smaller subgraphs retained for generations. G-Retriever \[[147](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib147)\] defines a semantic score for each retrieved node and edge, then refines the graph by solving the prize-collecting Steiner tree problem to construct a more compact and relevant subgraph.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I2.i2.p1.1.1">Syntactic-based pruning:</span> Syntactic-based pruning removes irrelevant nodes from a syntactic perspective. For instance, Su et al. \[[377](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib377)\] leverages dependency analysis to generate a parsing tree of the context and then filters the retrieved nodes based on their span distance from the parsing tree.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I2.i3.p1.1.1">Structure-based pruning:</span> Structure-based pruning methods focus on pruning the retrieved graph based on its structural properties. For example, RoK \[[431](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib431)\] filters out reasoning paths in the subgraph by calculating the average PageRank score for each path. Other works, such as Jiang et al. \[[181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181)\] and He et al. \[[144](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib144)\], also leverage PageRank to extract the most relevant entities.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I2.i4.p1.1.1">Dynamic pruning:</span> Unlike the aforementioned methods, which typically prune the graph once, dynamic pruning removes noisy nodes dynamically during training. For example, JointLK \[[382](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib382)\] uses attention weights to recursively remove irrelevant nodes at each layer, keeping only a fixed ratio of nodes. Similarly, DHLK \[[430](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib430)\] filters out nodes with attention scores below a certain threshold dynamically during the learning process.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.5.2 </span> Reranker

The performance of LLMs can be influenced by the position of relevant information within the context, whether it appears at the beginning, middle, or end \[[43](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib43)\]. Additionally, LLMs’ generation is impacted by the order in which in-context knowledge is provided, with later documents contributing less than earlier ones \[[172](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib172), [256](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib256)\]. While retrieved information is typically ordered by relevance scores during the retrieval process, these scores are often based on coarse-grained rankings across a large set of candidates. Enhancing reordering solely among the retrieved information at a fine-grained level, a process known as re-ranking, is essential to achieve optimal downstream performance. For example, Li et al. \[[234](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib234)\] rerank retrieved triples using a pre-trained cross-encoder. Jiang et al. \[[185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185)\] and Liu et al. \[[252](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib252)\] employ pre-trained reranker models to rerank retrieved paths. Yu et al. \[[498](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib498)\] train a GNN to rerank the retrieved passages. Liao et al. \[[246](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib246)\] order the paths by the time they occurred, giving more emphasis to recent paths.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.5.3 </span> Graph Augmentation

Graph augmentation aims to enrich the retrieved graph to either enhance the content or improve the robustness of the generator. This process can involve adding supplementary information to the retrieved graph, sourced from external data or knowledge embedded within LLMs. There are two main categories of methods:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I3.i1.p1.1.1">Graph Structure Augmentation:</span> Graph structure augmentation methods involve adding new nodes and edges to the retrieved graph. For instance, GraphQA \[[389](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib389)\] augments the retrieved subgraph by incorporating noun phrase chunk nodes extracted from the context. Moreover, Yasunaga et al. \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492)\] and Taunk et al. \[[389](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib389)\] treat the query as a node, integrating it into the retrieved graph to create direct connections between the query and relevant information. Tang et al. \[[388](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib388)\] augment the graph structure based on pretrained diffusion models.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I3.i2.p1.1.1">Graph Feature Augmentation:</span> Graph feature augmentation methods focus on enriching the features of the nodes and edges in the graph. Since the original features might be lengthy or sparse, data augmenters can be employed to summarize or provide additional details for these features. For example, Once \[[258](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib258)\] uses LLMs as Content Summarizers, User Profilers, and Personalized Content Generators in recommendation systems. Similarly, LLM-Rec \[[276](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib276)\] and KAR \[[458](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib458)\] apply various prompting techniques to enrich node features, making them more informative for downstream tasks.

Additionally, some graph augmentation techniques focus solely on the retrieved graph itself, such as randomly dropping nodes, edges, or features to improve model robustness. Ding et al. \[[86](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib86)\] provide a systematic review of these data augmentation methods.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.5.4 </span> Verbalizing

Verbalizing refers to converting retrieved triples, paths or graphs into natural language that can be consumed by LLMs. There are two main approaches to verbalization: linear verbalization and model-based verbalization.

Linear verbalization methods typically convert graphs into text using predefined rules. The primary techniques for linear verbalization include:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.1">Tuple-based:</span> These methods place the different pieces of retrieved information and order them in a tuple \[[14](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib14), [309](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib309)\]. For example, when performing retrieval on a KG, many methods retrieve a set of facts. A single fact is verbalized in the generation prompt as the tuple <math alttext="(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{relation}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3b"><vector id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.4.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3"><apply id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.1"><times xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2"><times id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.1.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.1"></times><ci id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.2a.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.2"><mtext id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.2.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.2">relation</mtext></ci><cn id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.2.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.1"></times><ci id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.2a.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.2"><mtext id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.2">entity</mtext></ci><cn id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3.3.3.3.3">2</cn></apply></vector></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3c">(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{relation}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I4.i1.p1.1.m1.3d">( entity 1 , relation 1 , entity 2 )</annotation></semantics></math> \[[308](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib308), [395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395)\]. For a set of facts, we first sort them in a specific order, and then verbalize them one at a time as an individual tuple. Each piece of information is typically separated by line in the prompt. Note that the same logic can be applied to paths, nodes, and so on.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.1">Template-based:</span> These methods verbalize paths or graphs using predefined templates to generate more natural text. For example, LLaGA \[[49](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib49)\] proposes some templates such as Hop-Field Overview Template to convert graph into sequence. For KGs, several methods \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134), [244](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib244)\] convert individual facts into natural text. For example, Guo et al. \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134)\] convert a fact (entity 1, relation, entity 2) to text using the template <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.2">“The </span> {<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.3">relation</span>} <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.4"> of </span> {<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.5">entity 1</span>} <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.6"> is/are: </span> {<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.7">entity 2</span>}<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.I4.i2.p1.1.8">”</span>.

Model-based verbalization methods typically use fine-tuned models or LLMs to convert input facts into coherent and natural language. These methods generally fall into two categories:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I5.i1.p1.1.1">Graph-to-text verbalization</span>: These methods focus on converting retrieved graphs into natural language while preserving all the information. For instance, Koncel-Kedziorski et al. \[[211](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib211)\] and Wang et al. \[[421](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib421)\] leverage graph transformers to generate text from knowledge graph. Ribeiro et al. \[[336](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib336)\] evaluate several pretrained language models for graph-to-text generation, while Wu et al. \[[455](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib455)\], and Agarwal et al. \[[3](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib3)\] fine-tune LLMs to transform graphs into sentences, ensuring a faithful representation of the graph content in textual form.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I5.i2.p1.1.1">Graph Summarization:</span> In contrast to Graph-to-Text Verbalization, which retains all details, Graph Summarization methods aim to generate concise summaries based on the retrieved graph and the query. EFSum \[[208](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib208)\] proposes two approaches: one directly prompts LLMs to summarize the retrieved facts and query, while the other fine-tunes LLMs specifically for summarization tasks. CoTKR \[[457](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib457)\], on the other hand, alternates between two operations: Reasoning, where it decomposes the question, generates a reasoning trace, and identifies the specific knowledge needed for the current step; and Summarization, where it summarizes the relevant knowledge from the subgraph that retrieved based on the current reasoning trace.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.6 </span> Generator

The generator aims to produce the desired output for specific tasks based on the query and the retrieved information. These tasks can range from discrimination tasks (e.g., node/edge/graph classification) to generation tasks (e.g., KG-based question answering) and graph generation (e.g., molecular generation). Due to the uniqueness of different tasks, different generators are often desired. We categorize generators into three main types: Discriminative-based Generators, which leverage models like GNNs and Graph Transformers for tasks like classification; LLM-based Generators, which utilize the capabilities of LLMs to generate answers for text-based tasks; and Graph-based Generators, which generate new graphs using generative models such as diffusion models. Next, we provide a detailed illustration of these generators.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.6.1 </span> Discrimination-based Generator

Discrimination-based generators focus on discriminative and regression tasks, which can typically be modeled as graph tasks, such as node, edge, or graph classification and regression. Models designed for graph data, such as GNNs and Graph Transformers, are widely used as discrimination-based generators. The choice of GNN depends on the graph type and task. For instance, GCN \[[206](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib206)\], GraphSAGE \[[138](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib138)\], and GAT \[[402](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib402)\] are typically applied to homogeneous graphs, whereas models like RGCN \[[350](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib350)\] and HAN \[[423](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib423)\] are used for heterogeneous graphs, and HGNN \[[111](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib111)\] and Hyper-Attention \[[16](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib16)\] are suitable for hypergraphs. Additionally, graph transformers \[[296](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib296), [354](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib354)\] have gained popularity for their ability to capture global dependencies. Additionally, different training strategies, such as (semi-)supervised learning \[[279](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib279)\] and graph contrastive learning \[[192](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib192), [264](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib264)\], are employed depending on the specific requirements of the task.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.6.2 </span> LLM-based Generator

LLMs have demonstrated remarkable capabilities in understanding and generating natural language across a wide range of tasks. However, LLMs are inherently designed to process sequential data, while the retrieved information in GraphRAG is typically structured as graphs. Although various GraphRAG organizers, such as verbalization methods, convert retrieved graph information into text, these transformations may result in the loss of important graph structure information, which could be crucial for certain tasks. To take advantage of the ability of LLMs, many research efforts have been proposed to feed the graph information into LLMs, and we summarize them into the following categories:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I6.i1.p1.1.1">Verbalizing:</span> Verbalizing aims to convert the retrieved information in GraphRAG into sequences that can be processed by LLMs. These methods are detailed in Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2.5.4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.SS5.SSS4 "2.5.4 Verbalizing ‣ 2.5 Organizer ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)").

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I6.i2.p1.1.1">Embedding-fusion:</span> Embedding-fusion integrates graph embeddings and text embeddings within LLMs. The graph embeddings can be obtained using GNNs or Graph Transformers\[[36](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib36)\]. To align graph embeddings with text embeddings, a domain projector is typically learned to map graph embeddings to the text embedding space. Embedding fusion can occur at different layers of LLMs. For example, He et al. \[[147](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib147)\] feed the projected graph embeddings through the self-attention layers of LLMs, while Tian et al. \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395)\] prepend the projected graph embeddings with the text tokens. \[[9](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib9)\] fuse the text and projected graph embeddings before the prediction layers of LLMs. Additionally, LLMs can either be fine-tuned along with the domain projector using methods such as LoRA, or the LLM can remain fixed, training only the graph embedding model and domain projector.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I6.i3.p1.1.1">Positional embedding-fusion:</span> Directly converting the graph into sequences by Verbalization may lose graph structure information, which can be crucial in some tasks. Positional embedding-fusion aims to add the position of nodes in the retrieved graph to the LLMs. GIMLET\[[549](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib549)\], as a unified graph-text model, employs a generalized position embedding to encode both graph structures and textual instructions as unified tokens. LINKGPT \[[148](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib148)\] leverages the pairwise encoding in LPFormer \[[361](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib361)\] to encode the pairwise information between two nodes.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">2.6.3 </span> Graph-based Generator

In the scientific graph domain, GraphRAG generators often go beyond LLM-based methods due to the need for accurate structure generation. RetMol \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433)\] is particularly versatile because it can work with various encoder and decoder architectures, supporting multiple generative models and molecule representations. For example, generators can be transformer-based or utilize Graph VAE architectures. Huang et al. \[[167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\] highlight the use of a diffusion model, specifically the 3D molecular diffusion model IRDIF. In the generation process, SE(3)-equivariance is achieved through architectures like Equivariant Graph Neural Networks (EGNNs) \[[349](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib349)\], which ensure that the geometric properties of molecular structures remain invariant to spatial transformations such as rotation, translation, and reflection at each step. Incorporating SE(3)-equivariance into the diffusion model guarantees that the generated molecular structures maintain geometric consistency under these transformations. For KGs, multiple works \[[110](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib110), [492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492), [389](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib389)\] use a GNN to generate the answer. The GNNs used in these works are conditional on the query, thereby making the final predictions relevant to it.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.7 </span> Graph Datasources

We have conducted a comprehensive review of the primary techniques applied in the initial four model-centric components of GraphRAG—namely, the query processor, retriever, organizer, and generator. However, even with the best configurations of these components, a GraphRAG system may still fall short of optimal performance if the underlying graph data sources, from which external knowledge is retrieved, are not meticulously curated. This also underscores the recent significant shift in AI research from a model-centric to a data-centric perspective, where enhancing data quality and relevance becomes equally, if not more, crucial for achieving superior results. Adopting this data-centric perspective, the following section provides an overview of existing GraphRAG research on constructing graph data sources from a high-level perspective, with a detailed discussion of domain-specific graph construction methods reserved for the subsequent domain-specific section.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I7.i1.p1.1.1">Explicit Construction:</span> Explicit construction refers to building graphs based on explicit and predefined relationships in the data. This method is widely adopted across various domains. For example, molecule graphs are constructed from the connections between atoms; knowledge graphs are formed based on explicit relationships between entities; citation graphs are built by linking papers through citation relationships; and recommendation graphs model interactions between users and items.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I7.i2.p1.1.1">Implicit Construction:</span> Implicit construction is used when there are no explicit relationships between nodes, but instead, implicit connections can be derived. For instance, word co-occurrence in a document can suggest shared semantic information, and feature interaction in Tabular data can indicate the correlation between features. Graphs can explicitly model these connections, which might be beneficial to the downstream tasks.

After the graph is constructed, there are also several ways to formally represent graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I8.i1.p1.3.1">Adjacency matrix:</span> The adjacency matrix is one of the most popular ways to denote a graph. Specifically, the adjacency matrix <math alttext="\mathbf{A}\in\mathbb{R}^{||\mathcal{V}\times|\mathcal{V}|}" class="ltx_math_unparsed" display="inline" id="S2.I8.i1.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics is the number of nodes.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.1">Edge list:</span> The edge list represents each edge in the graph, typically in the form of tuples or triples, such as <math display="inline" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S2.I8.i2.p1.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S2.I8.i2.p1.5.m5.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i2.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml">r</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml">i</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1b"><ci id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1.1">𝑖</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1c">i</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.6.m6.1d">italic_i</annotation></semantics></math> and <math alttext="j" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1a"><mi id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml">j</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1b"><ci id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1.1">𝑗</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1c">j</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I8.i2.p1.7.m7.1d">italic_j</annotation></semantics></math>.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.1">Adjacency list:</span> The adjacency list is a node-centric representation where each node is associated with a list of its neighbors. It is typically represented as a dictionary <math alttext="\{i:\mathcal{N}_{i}\}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2"><semantics id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2a"><mrow id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.cmml"><mo id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.2" stretchy="false" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.1.cmml">{</mo><mi id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">i</mi><mo id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.3" lspace="0.278em" rspace="0.278em" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.1.cmml">:</mo><msub id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.cmml"><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.2" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.2.cmml">𝒩</mi><mi id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.3" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.3.cmml">i</mi></msub><mo id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.4" stretchy="false" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.1.cmml">}</mo></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2b"><apply id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1"><csymbol cd="latexml" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.2.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.2">conditional-set</csymbol><ci id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑖</ci><apply id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.2">𝒩</ci><ci id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2.2.1.1.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2c">\{i:\mathcal{N}_{i}\}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.1.m1.2d">{ italic_i : caligraphic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT }</annotation></semantics></math>, where <math alttext="\mathcal{N}_{i}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1a"><msub id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">𝒩</mi><mi id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml">i</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.2">𝒩</ci><ci id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1.1.3">𝑖</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1c">\mathcal{N}_{i}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.2.m2.1d">caligraphic_N start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> is the neighbor list of node <math alttext="i" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1a"><mi id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">i</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1.1">𝑖</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1c">i</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.I8.i3.p1.3.m3.1d">italic_i</annotation></semantics></math>.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I8.i4.p1.1.1">Node Sequence:</span> A node sequence transforms a graph into a sequence of nodes in either an irreversible or reversible manner. Most serialization methods are irreversible and do not allow for complete recovery of the original graph structure. For example, there are also some serialization methods that are reversible which can recover the whole graph structure. For example, Zhao et al. \[[552](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib552)\] propose serializing graphs using Eulerian paths by first applying Eulerization to the graph. Besides, if the graph establishes a tree structure, the BFS/DFS can also serialize the graph in a reversible manner.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S2.I8.i5.p1.1.1">Nature language:</span> With the growing popularity of LLMs for processing text-based information, various methods have been developed to describe graphs using natural language.

Note that the above-mentioned data structures can only represent basic graphs without support for complex scenarios such as multi-relational edges or edge attributes. For instance, using an adjacency matrix to represent a multi-relational attributed graph requires an expanded structure: <math connecting node <math alttext="i" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS7.p4.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S2.SS7.p4.4.m4.1a"><mi id="S2.SS7.p4.4.m4.1.1" alttext="j" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1.1" xref="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1.1.cmml">j</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1b"><ci id="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS7.p4.5.m5.1.1">𝑗</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" display="inline" id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1.1" xref="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1.1.cmml">r</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1b"><ci id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1.1">𝑟</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1c">r</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS7.p4.6.m6.1d">italic_r</annotation></semantics></math>.

Selecting an appropriate graph representation is essential for task-specific requirements. For example, Ge et al. \[[122](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib122)\] finds that the order of graph descriptions significantly impacts LLMs’ comprehension of graph structures and their performance across different tasks.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">3 </span> Knowledge Graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_kg.png)

A knowledge graph is a structured database that connects entities through well-defined relationships. It can either encompass a broad spectrum of general knowledge, such as the widely recognized Google Knowledge Graph \[[53](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib53), [261](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib261)\], or delve deeply into specialized domains, like the BioASQ dataset \[[400](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib400)\] for biomedical reasoning. The diverse information contained in a knowledge graph – represented as entities, relationships, paths, and subgraphs – serves as a valuable resource for enhancing various downstream tasks across different sectors, including question-answering \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\], commonsense reasoning \[[169](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib169)\], fact-checking \[[201](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib201)\], recommender systems \[[131](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib131)\], drug discovery \[[28](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib28)\], healthcare \[[37](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib37)\], and fraud detection \[[287](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib287)\].

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.1 </span> Application Tasks

This section reviews representative applications that GraphRAG on KGs is used for.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Question-answering</span>: Question-answering (QA) can focus on a single domain or span across global knowledge. Typically, a query in text format is given, such as "What is the best way to predict a baby’s eye color?" or "Were there fossil fuels in the ground when humans evolved?" \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395)\] – the answer can be a sentence generated by a large language model (LLM), a selected text span from relevant documents, or even a specific choice in a multiple-choice QA scenario. In all these contexts, GraphRAG leverages knowledge graphs to retrieve relevant information, providing the necessary context or supporting facts to generate accurate answers.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Fact-Checking</span>: Fact-checking is to verify the truthfulness of statements by cross-referencing them with reliable sources of information. GraphRAG enhances this task by querying a knowledge graph to retrieve relevant facts and relational structures that either support or refute the given claim. GraphRAG identifies discrepancies or confirmations within the data by mapping the statement onto the knowledge graph, providing a thorough and evidence-based validation process.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Knowledge Graph Completion</span>: Knowledge graph completion is the task of predicting new facts to enhance the comprehensiveness of the graph and infer missing facts \[[535](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib535)\]. GraphRAG addresses this task by retrieving structural knowledge around the triplets for inference, supplying essential structural knowledge, and enhancing the LLM inference.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Cybersecurity Analysis and Defense</span>: Cybersecurity Analysis and Defense aims to analyze and respond to vulnerabilities, weaknesses, attack patterns, and threat tactics. With the increasing complexity and volume of cybersecurity data, GraphRAG has been proposed to provide cybersecurity analysis with more comprehensive insights into potential attack vectors and mitigation strategies \[[330](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib330)\].

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.2 </span> Knowledge Graph Construction

We discuss how KGs are typically constructed. For each type of construction technique, we give examples of common KG databases. How a KG is constructed is important, as it can affect both its usefulness and function in different downstream tasks. We describe the main techniques below:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I2.i1.p1.1.1">Manual construction</span>: Some KGs are constructed manually via human annotation. WikiData \[[404](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib404)\] is a KG that uses crowd-sourced efforts to gather a variety of knowledge. Each entity corresponds to a page in the Wikipedia encyclopedia. Another KG, the Unified Medical Language System (UMLS) \[[25](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib25)\], contains biomedical facts collected from numerous sources.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I2.i2.p1.1.1">Rule-based construction</span>: Many traditional approaches use rule-based techniques for constructing the graph. This takes the form of custom parsers and manually defined rules used to extract facts from raw text. Note that these parsers can differ depending on the source of the text. Prominent examples include ConceptNet \[[373](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib373)\], which links different words together via assertions, and Freebase \[[27](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib27)\] which contains a wide variety of general facts. REANO \[[106](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib106)\] extracts the entities and relations from a set of passages using traditional entity recognition (ER) and relation extraction (RE) methods, respectively. This includes SpaCy \[[151](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib151)\] for ER and TAGME \[[112](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib112)\] for RE. To extract the facts that connect two entities via a relation, they use DocuNet \[[526](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib526)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I2.i3.p1.1.1">LLM-based construction</span>: Recently, work has explored how LLMs can be used to construct KGs from a set of documents. In such a way, the LLM can automatically extract the entities and relations and link those together to form facts in the given text. Of note is that no ground-truth KG exists for these methods. Rather, they simply use a KG as a way to organize and represent a set of documents. For example, CuriousLLM \[[487](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib487)\] considers passages in the text as entities and determines whether two entities should be connected based on their encoded textual similarity. On the other hand, Cheng et al. \[[58](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib58)\] uses a manually-defined prompt to convert a piece of text into a KG. Graph-RAG \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\] first divides each document into chunks and then uses an LLM to detect all the entities in each chunk, including their name, type, and description. To identify the relation between any two entities, both entities and a description of their relationship are passed to an LLM. An LLM is then used again to summarize the content of each entity and relation to arrive at their final title. Lastly, AutoKG \[[40](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib40)\] uses a combination of LLM embeddings and clustering techniques to construct a KG from a set of texts.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.3 </span> Retriever

Real-world facts in KGs can provide grounded information for generative models, enhancing the reliability of the model output. Given the structured nature of KGs, they are naturally well-suited for retrieval. The goal is for a given question or query to retrieve either relevant facts <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote2"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">2</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">2</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">2</span>Throughout this paper, we will refer to facts as triples or edges, interchangeably</span></span></span> or entities that can help answer that question. Multiple considerations need to be considered during retrieval, including the type of facts we want to retrieve, the efficiency, and the amount of facts retrieved. In general, retrieval of KGs has two stages: identifying seed entities and retrieving facts or entities. We describe both below.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS3.p2.1.1">Identifying seed entities</span>: The first step in retrieving the relevant facts for a given query is to identify a set of “seed entities”, which we’ll refer to as <math alttext="V_{\text{seed}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1a"><msub id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mtext id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml">seed</mtext></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><ci id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3"><mtext id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3">seed</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1c">V_{\text{seed}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.SS3.p2.1.m1.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT seed end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>. Seed entities are the initial entities that are chosen to be highly relevant to the original query. Given such, we expect that triples that contain any of these entities or are nearby in the graph should provide helpful context. Multiple techniques exist for identifying the seed entities. Some works \[[181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181), [200](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib200), [251](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib251), [380](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib380), [522](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib522)\] assume that we are given a set of initial entities for each query. However, most works \[[110](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib110), [381](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib381), [443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443), [530](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib530), [308](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib308), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\] attempt to extract the entities from the query. One approach is through entity extraction \[[6](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib6)\], which uses methods specifically designed for extracting entities from a given text. Most works only extract entities from the original query. Another common approach is to extract a set of entities that are semantically similar to the original query \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443), [347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347)\]. HyKGE \[[185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185)\] first generates a hypothesis and extracts entities from the original query and the hypothesis. Similarly, in order to reduce the possibility of hallucination, Guo et al. \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134)\] uses an LLM to generate two similar questions and retrieves all entities found in the original and generated questions. In a similar vein, RoK \[[431](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib431)\] first uses chain of thoughts reasoning to expand the original query, extracting the seed entities from the expanded query.

<span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.SS3.p3.1.1">Retrieval Methods</span>: The outcome of the previous step provides us with a set of entities that are related in some capacity to the query. These entities are then leveraged to retrieve a set of facts or entities that can aid us in answering the query. We summarize the core retrieval methods below.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.1">Traversal-based retriever</span>: These methods traverse the graph and extract paths to aid in answering a specific question. Given the set of seed entities, <math alttext="V_{\text{seed}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1a"><msub id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mtext id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml">seed</mtext></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.3"><mtext id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.3">seed</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1c">V_{\text{seed}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT seed end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>, Yasunaga et al. \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492), [493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\], Zhang et al. \[[530](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib530)\] extract all paths up to length two between the entities in <math alttext="V_{\text{seed}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1a"><msub id="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.3"><mtext id="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.3">seed</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.2.m2.1c">V_{\text{seed}}</annotation><annotation class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.3.m3.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">V</mi><annotation-xml They further augment <math alttext="V" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml">V</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1.1">𝑉</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1c">V</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.4.m4.1d">italic_V</annotation></semantics></math> by including all triples that connect any two entities in <math alttext="V" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml">V</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1">𝑉</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1c">V</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.5.m5.1d">italic_V</annotation></semantics></math>. Given <math alttext="V" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml">V</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1">𝑉</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1c">V</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.6.m6.1d">italic_V</annotation></semantics></math>, both \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492), [530](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib530)\] only keep the top <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.7.m7.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> entities by relevance scores. This is calculated by training a separate model that takes the text embedding of the query and entity as input and outputs how relevant the entity is to the query. For Yasunaga et al. \[[493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\], if <math alttext="\lvert V\rvert&gt;200" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.cmml"><mrow id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.1.cmml"><mo id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.2.1" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.1.1.cmml">|</mo><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.1.cmml">V</mi><mo id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.2.2" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.1.1.cmml">|</mo></mrow><mo id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.1.cmml">&gt;</mo><mn id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.3" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.3.cmml">200</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2"><gt id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.1"></gt><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.2"><abs id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.2.2.1"></abs><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.1">𝑉</ci></apply><cn id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1.2.3">200</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1c">\lvert V\rvert&gt;200</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.8.m8.1d">| italic_V | &gt; 200</annotation></semantics></math>, they randomly sample 200 entities. Sun et al. \[[381](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib381)\] use a version of beam-search to explore the KG. Jiang et al. \[[185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185)\], Feng et al. \[[110](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib110)\] extract all paths of length <math alttext="k\leq 2" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.2.cmml">k</mi><mo id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.1.cmml">≤</mo><mn id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.3.cmml">2</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1"><leq id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.1"></leq><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.2">𝑘</ci><cn id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1.1.3">2</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1c">k\leq 2</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.9.m9.1d">italic_k ≤ 2</annotation></semantics></math> between seed entities. Alternatively, LARK \[[62](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib62)\] retrieves all facts that lie on paths <math alttext="\leq k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.2.cmml"></mi><mo id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.1.cmml">≤</mo><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.3.cmml">k</mi></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1"><leq id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.1"></leq><csymbol cd="latexml" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.2">absent</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1.1.3">𝑘</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1c">\leq k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.10.m10.1d">≤ italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> in length starting from the seed entities. Delile et al. \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\] first extract the shortest paths connecting all seed entities. They further prioritize some entities over others by considering the recency, importance, and relevance to the query of their associated text. OREOLM \[[158](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib158)\] traverse <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.11.m11.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> hops from the seed entities, contextualizing the importance of each relation and entity to a path via a learnable <math alttext="d" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1.1.cmml">d</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1.1">𝑑</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1c">d</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.12.m12.1d">italic_d</annotation></semantics></math>\-dimensional embedding and it’s LM-encoded representation. Zhang et al. \[[522](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib522)\] introduce a trainable retriever that traverses the graph starting from each seed entity. They also train a model to score each newly visited edge, only keeping a portion of them. KG-RAG \[[347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347)\] works in a similar manner, scoring each edge by its relevance and similarity to the query via a dense retriever. They then use an LLM to decide which paths to explore in the next step. RoG \[[271](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib271)\] uses instruction tuning to fine-tune an LLM to generate useful relation paths, which can be retrieved from the KG. KnowledgeNavigator \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134)\] first uses the query to predict the expected number of hops, <math alttext="h_{Q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1a"><msub id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.2.cmml">h</mi><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.3.cmml">Q</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.2">ℎ</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1.1.3">𝑄</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1c">h_{Q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.13.m13.1d">italic_h start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_Q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> needed to traverse in the retrieval stage. It then traverses <math alttext="h_{Q}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1a"><msub id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.2.cmml">h</mi><mi id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.3.cmml">Q</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.2">ℎ</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1.1.3">𝑄</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1c">h_{Q}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i1.p1.14.m14.1d">italic_h start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_Q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> hops starting from the seed entities, using an LLM to score and prune irrelevant nodes. Wu et al. \[[456](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib456)\] operate in a similar manner; however, they choose which paths to traverse based solely on the relations. Furthermore, when scoring a path, all relations that lie on that path are considered when computing the score. RoK \[[431](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib431)\] considers a different approach, using the Personalized PageRank (PPR) score to identify useful paths. They further augment these paths by including the 1-hop neighbors of the seed entities. PullNet \[[380](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib380)\] assumes that each entity has an associated set of documents. Given a single seed entity, PullNet, traverses k hops, where in each iteration it extracts the facts for the newly observed entities. It also extracts any entities that are contained in documents associated with an entity found in the traversal. Furthermore, for each entity, only the top N facts are used, which are ranked via similarity to the query. KG-R3 \[[312](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib312)\] uses MINERVA \[[75](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib75)\], a reinforcement learning approach to mining paths between entities, to retrieve a set of important paths between both entities in the fact. Wang et al. \[[428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\] use an LLM to traverse the graph starting from the seed entities. At each iteration in the traversal, we choose the next node to visit by prompting an LLM. Specifically, given the information already collected in the traversal, the LLM is prompted to generate the remaining information needed to correctly answer the question. The neighboring node that best matches the required information is chosen as the next node to visit. They further instruction-tune the LLM.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.1">Subgraph-based retriever</span>: These methods extract a subgraph of size <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> around each of the seed entities. Facts that contain one of the seed entities or are nearby, should be highly relevant to answering the question. Furthermore, they may actually contain the answer itself. Each of \[[308](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib308), [395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181), [205](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib205)\] extract either the one or two hop subgraph around each seed entity. The final set of facts is the union of each individual subgraph. Gao et al. \[[118](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib118)\] propose to first extract the subgraph containing the seed entity and potential answers using the method in Sun et al. \[[379](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib379)\]. This is then partitioned into a set of smaller subgraphs. Then, they design a framework to rank the subgraphs, keeping the top <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> subgraphs for generation. For a question-choice pair, MVP-Tuning \[[164](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib164)\] considers the triple that contains the highest number of seed and choice entities. They further augment this by extracting the top <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i2.p1.3.m3.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> most similar questions in the dataset using BM25 \[[338](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib338)\], and extract the triples for each of them.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.1">Rule-based retriever</span>: These methods use pre-defined rules or templates to extract paths from the graph. GenTKG \[[246](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib246)\] considers a temporal KG, where they first extract logical rules from the KG, and use the top k rules to extract paths in a given time interval for the seed entities. Both \[[72](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib72), [270](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib270)\] generate queries using SPARQL, which are then used to retrieve import paths. KEQING \[[406](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib406)\] decomposes the original query into <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i3.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> sub-queries using using an LLM fine-tuned via LoRA \[[153](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib153)\]. For each sub-query, they find the most similar question templates, which are predefined. For each template, they further pre-define a set of logical chains, which are then used to extract matching paths for the seed entities in the sub-query from the KG.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.1">GNN-based retriever</span>: GNN-RAG \[[289](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib289)\] trains a GNN for the retrieval task. A separate round of message passing is done for each query <math alttext="q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">q</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑞</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1c">q</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_q</annotation></semantics></math>, which is incorporated in the message computation along with the relation and entity representations. The GNN is then trained as in the node classification task, where the correct answer entity for <math alttext="q" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">q</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝑞</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1c">q</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_q</annotation></semantics></math> has a label of 1 and 0 otherwise. During inference, the entities with probability above some threshold are treated as candidate answers, and the shortest path from the seed entity is extracted. Liu et al. \[[251](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib251)\] use a conditional GNN \[[163](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib163)\] for retrieval, where for each query, only the seed entity (they assume there is only one) is initialized to a non-zero representation based on the LLM-encoded query. They then run <math alttext="L" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">L</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1.1">𝐿</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1c">L</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.3.m3.1d">italic_L</annotation></semantics></math> rounds of message passing where after each layer <math alttext="l" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i4.p1.4.m4.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i4.p1.4.m4.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml">l</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.4.m4.1b"><ci encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.4.m4.1d">italic_l</annotation></semantics></math> only the top-K new edges are kept, resulting in a set of entities <math alttext="C_{q}^{l}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.5.m5.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i4.p1.5.m5.1a"><msubsup id="S3.I3.i4.p1.5.m5.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i4.p1.5.m5.1.1.2.2" This is determined by a learnable attention weight, which prunes the other edges from the graph. The final set of candidate entities is the union of candidate entities at each layer <math alttext="l" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i4.p1.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.1.3">𝐿</ci></apply><apply id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.2"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.2.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.2.2.3">𝑞</ci></apply><ci id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1.1.3.2.3">𝑙</ci></apply></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1c">C_{q}=\cup_{l=1}^{L}C_{q}^{l}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i4.p1.7.m7.1d">italic_C start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT = ∪ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_l = 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_L end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_C start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_q end_POSTSUBSCRIPT start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_l end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>. It is optimized in a similar manner to \[[289](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib289)\]. For each candidate entity, they retrieve an evidence chain by backtracking from the entity until it reaches the seed entity, choosing those edges with the highest attention weight. REANO \[[106](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib106)\] initializes the entity and relation representations via the mean-pooled representations of all mentions of that entity/relation in the texts, encoded by T5. They then run a GNN, which includes an attention weight that considers the relevance of a given triple to the original question (also encoded by T5). After running the GNN, they retrieve the top K triples in the KG that are most relevant to the question, where relevance is defined via the dot product between the triple encoded by the GNN and the question.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i5.p1.1.1">Similarity-based retriever</span>: STaRK \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\] considers the vector similarity of the query to each entity. Each entity embeds both the textual and relational information together. They further consider multi-vector similarity, where the entities are encoded using multiple vectors. This is done by chunking the textual and relational information of each entity, with each chunk being embedded into its own vector. Both REALM \[[567](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib567)\] and EMERGE \[[566](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib566)\] extract the entities most similar to the query. While REALM only retrieves the entities themselves, EMERGE further retrieves the 1-hop subgraph around each entity.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.1">Relation-based retriever</span>: Kim et al. \[[200](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib200)\] propose a general framework for reasoning on KGs using LLMs. They first use an LLM to segment the original query into a set of <math alttext="i\in I" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics Given the set of <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i6.p1.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i6.p1.6.m6.1.1" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.6.m6.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> relations, for each sub-sentence, they retrieve all triples that contain a relation in <math alttext="\mathcal{R}_{i,k}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2"><semantics id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2a"><msub id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2.3" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2.3.cmml"><mi class="ltx_font_mathcaligraphic" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2.2.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2.2.2.4"><ci id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.1.1.1.1">𝑖</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2.2.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2.2.2.2">𝑘</ci></list></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2c">\mathcal{R}_{i,k}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.7.m7.2d">caligraphic_R start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i , italic_k end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> and whose entities are in <math alttext="\bigcup_{i\in I}S_{i}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.cmml"><msub xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.3.cmml">i</mi></msub></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1"><apply id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><union id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.2"></union><apply id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.3"><in id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.3.1.cmml"
