xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.1.3.3">𝐼</ci></apply></apply><apply id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.2">𝑆</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1.1.2.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1c">\bigcup_{i\in I}S_{i}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i6.p1.8.m8.1d">⋃ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i ∈ italic_I end_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_S start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>. GenTKGQA \[[119](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib119)\] focuses on temporal KG QA. Like \[[200](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib200)\], they retrieve the top-k relations for the query. They then retrieve all facts that contain one of the top k relations and satisfy the temporal constraints.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.12.1">Fusion-based retriever</span>: These techniques consider a combination of different retrieval techniques. Mindmap \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443)\] considers extracting some paths <math alttext="\leq k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml"></mi><mo id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">≤</mo><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml">k</mi></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1"><leq id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.1"></leq><csymbol cd="latexml" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.2">absent</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1.1.3">𝑘</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1c">\leq k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.1.m1.1d">≤ italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> hops from the seed entity and the 1-hop subgraph of each seed. These two extracted components are combined into one subgraph. DALK \[[224](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib224)\] uses a procedure similar to Mindmap, where they extract both paths and the 1-hop subgraph around each seed entity. However, they argue that this procedure often results in the retrieval of redundant or unnecessary information. To remove these facts, they use an LLM to rank the retrieved facts given both the original question and the subgraph. Only the Top-k most relevant facts are kept. UniOQA \[[244](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib244)\] considers two branches for retrieving. The first is a translator, which is a fine-tuned LLM that generates the answer in a CQL format. the second is a searcher that retrieves the 1-hop subgraph around the seed entities. When determining the answer, answers from the translator are prioritized over those from the searcher. KG-Rank \[[483](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib483)\] considers ranking all triples in the 1-hop neighborhood of the seed entities via the similarity of the relation to the query, the similarity of each triple to the encoded output of <math alttext="a=\text{LLM}(q)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.2.cmml">a</mi><mo id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.1.cmml">=</mo><mrow id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.cmml"><mtext id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.2a.cmml">LLM</mtext><mo id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mrow id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.3.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.cmml"><mo id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.3.2.1" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.cmml">(</mo><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">q</mi><mo id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.3.2.2" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.cmml">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2"><eq id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.1"></eq><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.2">𝑎</ci><apply id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3"><times id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.1"></times><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.2a.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.2"><mtext id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.2.3.2">LLM</mtext></ci><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝑞</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1c">a=\text{LLM}(q)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_a = LLM ( italic_q )</annotation></semantics></math>, and an MMR ranking \[[35](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib35)\] that uses the similarity score. Only the top-ranked triples are kept. GrapeQA \[[389](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib389)\] extends \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492)\] by further including a set of “extra nodes”, which are the common neighbors of the entities retrieved via a path-based retriever. They further introduce a clustering-based method for pruning entities that may be irrelevant to the query. SubgraphRAG \[[232](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib232)\] considers both GNN and textual information. For the GNN, they consider initializing the node representations using a one-hot encoding to differentiate between seed entities and others. A GNN is then run for L layers, resulting in the final representation <math alttext="s_{v}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1a"><msub id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml">s</mi><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.3" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml">v</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.2">𝑠</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1.1.3">𝑣</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1c">s_{v}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.3.m3.1d">italic_s start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_v end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> for a node <math alttext="v" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml">v</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1.1">𝑣</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1c">v</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.4.m4.1d">italic_v</annotation></semantics></math>. To retrieve the relevant triples, they consider first concatenating the final node representations for each triple <math alttext="(h,r,t)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i7.p1.5.m5.3"><semantics id="S3.I3.i7.p1.5.m5.3a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i7.p1.5.m5.3.4.2" xref="S3.I3.i7.p1.5.m5.3.4.1.cmml"><mo id="S3.I3.i7.p1.5.m5.3.4.2.1" stretchy="false" Only the top K triples are chosen.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i8.p1.1.1">Agent-based retriever</span>: These techniques use LLM agents to retrieve facts from the KG. KnowledGPT \[[425](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib425)\] defines a set of tools for searching over a KG. Given a query, they generate a piece of code to search over the KG that considers the seed entities. The code is then executed over the KG to find the correct answer. KG-Agent \[[182](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib182)\] focuses on fine-tuning an LLM to generate the SQL code for retrieving the correct answer. Using a set of tools, they extract a set of paths that contain the seed entities. KnowAgent \[[568](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib568)\] first identifies the relevant actions for the query via a planning module. Using these actions, they then generate a set of paths that are used for generation.

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.1">Other retrievers</span>: KICGPT \[[439](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib439)\] is concerned with the task of knowledge graph completion, where given a partial fact <math alttext="(h,r,*)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.2" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.1.cmml"><mo id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.2.1" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.1.cmml">(</mo><mi id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml">h</mi><mo xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.1.cmml">)</mo></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3b"><vector id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.4.2"><ci xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3.3"></times></vector></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3c">(h,r,*)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.1.m1.3d">( italic_h , italic_r , ∗ )</annotation></semantics></math>, we want to predict the correct entity <math alttext="\hat{e}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.2.m2.1a"><mover accent="true" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.2.m2.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i8.p2.2.m2.1.1.2" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.2.m2.1d">over^ start_ARG italic_e end_ARG</annotation></semantics></math>. KICGPT retrieves the entities by first scoring all possible entities using a traditional KG embedding score function. That is, for a score function <math alttext="f(\cdot)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.3.m3.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i8.p2.3.m3.1.2" partial fact <math alttext="(h,r,*)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.4.m4.3"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.4.m4.3a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i8.p2.4.m4.3.4.2" They use RotatE \[[383](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib383)\] for the function <math alttext="f(\cdot)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1a"><mrow id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.cmml"><mi id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.2" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.2.cmml">f</mi><mo id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.1" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mrow id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.3.2" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.cmml"><mo id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.3.2.1" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.cmml">(</mo><mo id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.1" lspace="0em" rspace="0em" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.1.cmml">⋅</mo><mo id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.3.2.2" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.cmml">)</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1b"><apply id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2"><times id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.1"></times><ci id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.2.2">𝑓</ci><ci id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1.1">⋅</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1c">f(\cdot)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.6.m6.1d">italic_f ( ⋅ )</annotation></semantics></math>, a popular approach. Only the top <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.7.m7.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> entities by score are retrieved. To supplement their knowledge, they also retrieve all triples with (a) the same relation as the query and (b) all triples that contain the entity <math alttext="h" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1"><semantics id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1a"><mi id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1.1" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1.1.cmml">h</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1b"><ci id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1.1">ℎ</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1c">h</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I3.i8.p2.8.m8.1d">italic_h</annotation></semantics></math> in the query. These are referred to as the analogous and supplement triple pools, respectively.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.4 </span> Organizer

In this subsection, we describe how the retrieved knowledge is organized for generation. More concretely, this is how the information is formatted when given to the generator. Note that not every method necessarily has an explicit organizer. We summarize the common methods below:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.1">Tuple-based organizer</span>: These methods consider each piece of retrieved information as an ordered triple. For example, it would include a triple in the generation prompt as “<math alttext="(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{relation}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2)" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" Similarly, a path of length <math alttext="m" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1a"><mi id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">m</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1">𝑚</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1c">m</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.2.m2.1d">italic_m</annotation></semantics></math> is given by “<math alttext="(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{relation}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2,\text{relation}\&gt;2,%

encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.3.m3.6c">(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{relation}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2,\text{relation}\&gt;2,% \cdots,\text{entity}\&gt;m)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.3.m3.6d">( entity 1 , relation 1 , entity 2 , relation 2 , ⋯ , entity italic_m )</annotation></semantics></math>”. The entities and relations are usually represented either as their names or IDs. Each triple or path is usually listed on a separate line. Many works append the retrieved paths to the original query as additional context \[[381](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib381), [185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185), [289](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib289), [347](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib347), [271](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib271), [62](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib62), [251](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib251), [431](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib431), [568](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib568)\]. Other works that retrieve facts instead of paths operate in a similar manner, where instead they append the triples \[[308](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib308), [567](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib567), [72](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib72), [246](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib246), [483](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib483), [181](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib181), [200](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib200), [119](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib119)\]. Some methods \[[566](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib566), [395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395)\] consider only including the retrieved entities as the context. Given a set of facts, KG-R3 \[[312](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib312)\] first lists all entities and then relations, i.e., “<math alttext="(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2,\cdots,\text{entity}\&gt;m,\cdots,\text{%

xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.2.2">⋯</ci><apply id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4"><times id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.1"></times><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.2a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.2"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.2">relation</mtext></ci><cn id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.7.7.4.4.3">1</cn></apply><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.3.3.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.3.3">⋯</ci><apply id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5"><minus id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.1"></minus><apply id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2"><times id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.1"></times><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.2a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.2"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.2">relation</mtext></ci><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.2.3">𝑚</ci></apply><cn id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8.8.5.5.3">1</cn></apply></vector></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8c">(\text{entity}\&gt;1,\text{entity}\&gt;2,\cdots,\text{entity}\&gt;m,\cdots,\text{% relation}\&gt;1,\cdots,\text{relation}\&gt;m-1)</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.4.m4.8d">( entity 1 , entity 2 , ⋯ , entity italic_m , ⋯ , relation 1 , ⋯ , relation italic_m - 1 )</annotation></semantics></math>”. Delile et al. \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\] consider a KG where each entity has an associated chunk of text. Each text chunk for an entity is considered as a different piece of information to be included in the context. Both \[[395](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib395), [119](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib119)\] represent each entity and relation as an embedding, which is the combination of the LLM and GNN embedding. Liu et al. \[[251](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib251)\] further include the probability of each path containing the correct answer given by the GNN model. MVP-Tuning \[[164](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib164)\] considers combining multiple facts that share the same subject and relation to remove redundant information. That is, for a subject-relation pair <math alttext="(\text{subject},\text{relation})" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2"><semantics id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2a"><mrow id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.1.cmml"><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.2.1" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.1.cmml">(</mo><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1a.cmml">subject</mtext><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.2.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.1.cmml">,</mo><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.2a.cmml">relation</mtext><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.2.3" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.1.cmml">)</mo></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2b"><interval closure="open" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.3.2"><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.1.1">subject</mtext></ci><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.2a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.2"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2.2">relation</mtext></ci></interval></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2c">(\text{subject},\text{relation})</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.5.m5.2d">( subject , relation )</annotation></semantics></math>, they denote the facts for <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1"><semantics id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1a"><mi id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1b"><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.6.m6.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> possible objects as “<math alttext="\text{subject}\&gt;\text{relation}\&gt;\{\text{object}\&gt;1,\cdots,\text{object}\&gt;k\}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3"><semantics id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3a"><mrow id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.cmml"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.4" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.4a.cmml">subject</mtext><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.3" lspace="0.220em" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.3.cmml">⁢</mo><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.5" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.5a.cmml">relation</mtext><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.3a" lspace="0.220em" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.3.cmml">⁢</mo><mrow id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.3.cmml"><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.3" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.3.cmml">{</mo><mrow id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.cmml"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.2a.cmml">object</mtext><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mn id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.3" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.3.cmml"> 1</mn></mrow><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.4" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.3.cmml">,</mo><mi id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1" mathvariant="normal" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml">⋯</mi><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.5" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.3.cmml">,</mo><mrow id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.cmml"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.2" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.2a.cmml">object</mtext><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.1" lspace="0.220em" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.3" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.3.cmml">k</mi></mrow><mo id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.6" stretchy="false" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.3.cmml">}</mo></mrow></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3b"><apply id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3"><times id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.3.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.3"></times><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.4a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.4"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.4.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.4">subject</mtext></ci><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.5a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.5"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.5.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.5">relation</mtext></ci><set id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2"><apply id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1"><times id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.1"></times><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.2a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.2"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.2">object</mtext></ci><cn id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.2.2.1.1.1.3">1</cn></apply><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.1.1">⋯</ci><apply id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2"><times id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.1"></times><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.2a.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.2"><mtext id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.2.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.2">object</mtext></ci><ci id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.3.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3.3.2.2.2.3">𝑘</ci></apply></set></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3c">\text{subject}\&gt;\text{relation}\&gt;\{\text{object}\&gt;1,\cdots,\text{object}\&gt;k\}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i1.p1.7.m7.3d">subject relation { object 1 , ⋯ , object italic_k }</annotation></semantics></math>”. KG-Agent \[[182](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib182)\] stores the current KG information and the historical reasoning programs in lists.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.1">Text organizer</span>: Wu et al. \[[456](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib456)\] verbalize the retrieved subgraph by passing each triple to the LLM and prompting it to convert it to a text representation. MindMap Wen et al. \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443)\] uses a similar procedure for subgraphs, where each is organized as a path before being passed to the LLM. Some methods use a set of pre-defined templates to verbalize the triples or paths \[[134](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib134), [244](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib244), [205](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib205)\]. Wang et al. \[[406](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib406)\] experiment with verbalizing either via an LLM or pre-defined question templates, finding that LLM-based verbalizing works better for ChatGPT while template-based works better for LLaMA \[[398](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib398)\]. KICGPT \[[439](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib439)\] uses a combination of data preprocessing and LLM prompting to convert the triples to text. StaRK \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\] uses an LLM to synthesize each entity with its relational and textual information. Note that they use some pre-defined templates that depend on the specific task. CoTKR \[[457](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib457)\] uses an LLM to summarize and then re-write a subgraph of facts for a question through a “knowledge rewriter”. To train the rewriter, preference alignment is used, which optimizes the rewriter’s output to match our preferred output. First, <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i2.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> representations of the retrieved subgraph are produced, with ChatGPT choosing the best and work representations as the most and least preferred solutions.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I4.i3.p1.1.1">Other organizer</span>: There are some exceptions to the previous classification. KnowledGPT \[[425](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib425)\] represents the information in the form of a python class format. They also experiment with including additional information like the entity description and entity-aspect information.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.1">Re-Ranking</span>: Some methods also <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.2">re-rank</span> the information in a specific order. This is done as the order of information can have a subtle impact on LLM performance. Delile et al. \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\] order the text chunks of each entity based on the impact (measured by # of citations of the parent paper) and the recency. Dai et al. \[[72](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib72)\] sort the triples by the relevancy score to the triple. Choudhary and Reddy \[[62](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib62)\] attempt to order the paths in a logical matter, such that for a given path, the subsequent paths build upon it. Yang et al. \[[483](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib483)\] re-rank the retrieved triples using a task-specific Cross-Encoder \[[187](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib187)\]. STaRK \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\] considers re-ranking the retrieved entities using an LLM. The LLM is given the relational and textual information of each, and is asked to give it a score from 0 to 1, which is then used for re-ranking. GenTKG \[[246](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib246)\] orders the paths by the time they occurred, further including the time with each. KICGPT \[[439](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib439)\] ranks all entities using the score of the KG embedding score function, keeping only the top <math alttext="k" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">k</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1.1">𝑘</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1c">k</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.I4.i4.p1.1.m1.1d">italic_k</annotation></semantics></math> entities. KICGPT re-rank the entities using in-context learning, where they prompt the LLM with examples from the analogy and supplement pool, as prior knowledge to aid the LLM in how to re-rank the entities.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.5 </span> Generator

In this section we describe how the retrieved and organized data is used to generate a final response to the query. We categorize these generators according to the type of methods used to create these responses.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I5.i1.p1.1.1">LLM-based generator</span>: The vast majority of works use a LLM to generate the response. The input to the LLM is the original query and retrieved and organized context, formatted using a specific template. The most commonly used LLMs include ChatGPT \[[310](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib310)\], Gemini \[[390](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib390)\], Mistral \[[180](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib180)\], Gemma \[[391](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib391)\], among others. For open-source models where the weights are publicly available, fine-tuning is sometimes used to modify the weights for a specific task \[[244](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib244), [568](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib568)\]. This is often done through LoRA \[[153](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib153)\], which allows for efficient fine-tuning.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I5.i2.p1.1.1">GNN-based generator</span>: Some methods use graph neural networks (GNNs) \[[207](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib207)\] to conduct the generation. Yasunaga et al. \[[492](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib492)\], Taunk et al. \[[389](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib389)\], Feng et al. \[[110](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib110)\] extract both the language and GNN embeddings for each potential answer (i.e., entity) conditional on the query. The probability of a single entity being the answer is then learnt based on the fusion of the two types of embeddings.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I5.i3.p1.1.1">Other generators</span>:  Zhang et al. \[[530](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib530)\], Yasunaga et al. \[[493](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib493)\], Hu et al. \[[158](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib158)\] formulate the prediction as a masked language modeling (MLM) problem. The goal is to predict the correct value (i.e., entity) for the masked token which answers the query. To do so, they fine-tune RoBERTa \[[263](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib263)\] language model. KG-R3 \[[312](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib312)\] scores the potential answer entities by performing cross-attention the representations of the query and each individual entity. PullNet \[[380](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib380)\] uses GraftNet \[[379](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib379)\] to score the different entities. Gao et al. \[[118](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib118)\] first selects the correct subgraph by computing the cosine similarity between the query and subgraph representations. For the subgraph with the highest similarity, it’s fed to GraftNet \[[379](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib379)\] to select the most probable entity. REANO \[[106](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib106)\] passes the encoded triples and their associated text passages to the T5 decoder. The task is framed as a classification problem, where the goal is to assign the highest probability to the triple with the correct answer.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.6 </span> Resources and Tools

In this section, we list common tools and KGs that are used in graph RAG systems. For each, we give a brief description and a link to the project.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">3.6.1 </span> Data Resources

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I6.i1.p1.1.1">Freebase</span> <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote3"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">3</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">3</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">3</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://developers.google.com/freebase" title="">https://developers.google.com/freebase</a></span></span></span> \[[27](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib27)\] is an encyclopedic KG that contains a large variety of general and basic facts.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I6.i2.p1.1.1">ConceptNet</span> <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote4"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">4</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">4</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">4</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://conceptnet.io/" title="">https://conceptnet.io/</a></span></span></span> \[[373](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib373)\] is a semantic graph, where the links in the graph are used to describe the meaning of different words or ideas.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I6.i3.p1.1.1">WikiData</span> <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote5"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">5</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">5</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">5</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Wikidata:Main_Page" title="">https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Wikidata:Main_Page</a></span></span></span> \[[404](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib404)\] is a crowdsourced knowledge base that functions as a structured analog to the Wikipedia encyclopedia.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">3.6.2 </span> Tools

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I7.i1.p1.1.1">Graph RAG</span> <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote6"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">6</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">6</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">6</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://github.com/microsoft/graphrag" title="">https://github.com/microsoft/graphrag</a></span></span></span> \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\] is an official open-source implementation of the Graph RAG \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\] framework. It can further be installed via the <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S3.I7.i1.p1.1.2">graphrag</span> python package.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S3.I7.i2.p1.1.1">LangChain</span> <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote7"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">7</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">7</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">7</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://python.langchain.com/v0.1/docs/use_cases/graph/constructing/" title="">https://python.langchain.com/v0.1/docs/use_cases/graph/constructing/</a></span></span></span> is an open-source framework for using LLMs with various components and applications, including RAG, where using RAG on KGs is supportive.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">4 </span> Document Graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_doc.png)

A document graph typically models the connections between different documents or various granularity of documents. It is widely observed in real-world scenarios \[[351](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib351), [367](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib367), [471](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib471)\], such as hyperlinks connecting different websites and citations linking one paper to another. Additionally, a document graph’s relationships between sentences and entities can explicitly capture semantic and syntactic contextual information. The structural information in documents can serve as a valuable resource for GraphRAG, aiding LLMs in various tasks. For example, in the retrieval process in RAG, the document containing the answer may have less apparent connections with the question, such as in multi-hop question answering \[[288](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib288)\]. However, we can identify these relevant documents through their connections to other documents whose context is strongly aligned with the question’s context \[[90](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib90)\]. In this section, we will systematically review the document graph.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.1 </span> Application Tasks

Document graphs are beneficial for a wide range of tasks. In this subsection, we will review the tasks where document graphs can play a significant role. Although document graphs have not yet been fully integrated with LLMs, they still offer great potential for enhancing the capabilities of LLMs in various applications.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Multi-document Summarization(MDS)</span>: Multi-document summarization aims to condense the contents of multiple documents into a cohesive summary. Summarizing an entire corpus can involve large volumes of text, often exceeding the context window limitations of LLMs. Document graphs can help compress the corpus by extracting key components and their relationships, proving highly beneficial for MDS \[[491](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib491), [408](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib408), [237](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib237), [519](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib519), [462](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib462), [515](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib515), [482](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib482), [231](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib231), [45](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib45)\]. These graphs also provide different levels of granularity for summarization through hierarchical clustering \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Text generation</span>: Text generation focuses on producing coherent and meaningful text. While text-based RAG models have been widely used to generate more reliable text, document graphs can further enhance this process by retrieving similar documents using graph topology. For instance, when writing the abstract of a paper, access to its cited papers in the related work section can significantly improve writing efficiency, as these referenced papers often contain relevant knowledge and context \[[429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429), [313](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib313)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Document Retrieval</span>: Document retrieval aims to find a list of documents relevant to a given query, which is a key task in Information Retrieval (IR). The exact query terms may not always appear together in the candidate document; however, by leveraging the connections between documents, we can retrieve related documents through those documents that are strongly linked to the query. Thus, it becomes essential to consider document-level relationships in the retrieval process. Several works \[[90](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib90), [501](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib501), [544](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib544), [249](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib249)\] have leveraged document graphs with various granularities to improve document retrieval and ranking. Rather than retrieving the entire document, graphs can also be used to retrieve specific segments, such as chunks.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Document Classification:</span> Document classification is a fundamental task in natural language processing. Traditional methods often focus on the locality of words, limiting their ability to capture long-distance and non-consecutive word interactions. Moreover, these methods typically focus on individual documents, overlooking the relationships between them, where connected documents often exhibit homophily, meaning they are more likely to share similar labels. Building a graph can help enhance document classification by leveraging both local and global relationships between words within a single document \[[538](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib538), [259](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib259)\] and by utilizing document-level relationships \[[518](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib518), [461](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib461)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I1.i5.p1.1.1">Question Answering</span>: Question answering aims to provide answers to questions based on information from documents and is a fundamental task for RAG. However, documents used for question answering can be long, and traditional methods often focus on local structures within these documents, neglecting their global structure, which is crucial for long-range understanding. Additionally, some multi-hop questions require reasoning across multiple documents, necessitating the use of document-level relationships. Humans often consolidate scattered information into structured knowledge to streamline the reasoning process and make more accurate judgments, in line with cognitive load theory \[[384](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib384), [243](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib243)\]. Graph-based methods are well-suited for this task by constructing word-level document graphs \[[307](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib307), [410](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib410)\], utilizing document-level relationships \[[147](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib147), [428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\] and leveraging hierarchical interactions \[[108](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib108)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I1.i6.p1.1.1">Relation Extraction:</span> Relation extraction aims to extract semantic relationships between entities in text, which often requires local, global, syntactic, and semantic dependencies, especially in the case of document-level relation extraction. Graphs have been proven to be helpful for document-level relation extraction \[[409](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib409), [302](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib302), [344](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib344), [63](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib63), [558](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib558)\] by capturing these dependencies more effectively.

    In addition to the aforementioned tasks, graphs have also been shown to be helpful in other areas, such as fake news detection \[[155](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib155)\], coherence assessment \[[260](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib260)\], and machine translation \[[471](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib471)\].

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.2 </span> Document Graph Construction

Different tasks may require different types of document graphs, such as document-level, sentence-level, or word-level graphs. Therefore, the method of obtaining the document graph plays a crucial role. There are primarily two approaches to constructing a document graph: explicit construction and implicit construction.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i1.p1.1.1">Explicit Construction.</span> In real-world scenarios, many documents have explicit connections. Examples include web pages linked through hyperlinks, academic papers citing other works, and social media posts connected by reposts, comments, and interactions. In these cases, it is natural to connect the documents to build a document graph, as the connected documents often share semantic relationships \[[320](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib320)\]. For instance, Asai et al. \[[10](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib10)\] constructed a Wikipedia graph based on hyperlinks between Wikipedia articles. Li et al. \[[240](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib240), [239](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib239)\] follow the same way to leverage hyperlinks to construct graphs. Yu et al. \[[498](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib498)\] built a graph using an external knowledge graph (KG), where each node represents a retrieved passage mapped to entities within the KG, and two passage nodes are connected if their mapped entities are linked in the KG. These connections have been leveraged to pretrain large language models (LLMs), enhancing their performance across various tasks \[[494](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib494), [572](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib572), [157](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib157)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.p1.1.1">Implicit Construction.</span> Despite the presence of explicit connections between documents, different components within those documents also exhibit important semantic and syntactic relationships. The structure of these components can be highly beneficial for various tasks in natural language processing \[[450](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib450), [294](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib294)\]. For example, semantic parsing graphs, such as Abstract Meaning Representation (AMR) graphs, can be leveraged to enhance information extraction \[[546](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib546), [162](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib162)\] and text summarization \[[88](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib88)\]. Additionally, syntactic parsing trees are exploited to understand grammatical structure and resolve ambiguities \[[533](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib533)\].

    The implicit construction of document graphs is highly diverse, adapting to the specific requirements of different tasks. For instance, nodes in document graphs can represent various granularity, such as words, entities, sentences, text segments, paragraphs, documents, or topics. In addition, document graphs often exhibit heterogeneity, where edges connect different types of nodes. Next, we will detail the construction of different types of edges:

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i1.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Word-word edge:</span> The word-word edges connect words with semantic or syntactic relations or dependencies. There are various methods to establish these connections, including word co-occurrence within a sliding window \[[408](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib408), [501](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib501), [518](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib518), [249](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib249), [76](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib76)\], dependency parsing graphs \[[470](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib470), [386](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib386), [471](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib471), [516](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib516), [326](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib326), [401](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib401)\] generated by NLP parsing tools \[[219](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib219), [216](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib216)\], Abstract Meaning Representation \[[476](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib476), [546](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib546), [162](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib162), [88](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib88), [475](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib475)\] and semantic graphs \[[90](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib90), [370](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib370), [313](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib313)\] based on different representations \[[17](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib17), [359](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib359)\]. Additional techniques include coreference resolution \[[471](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib471), [344](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib344), [222](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib222), [82](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib82), [369](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib369)\], word embedding similarity \[[410](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib410), [415](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib415)\] and using large language models (LLMs) to extract relationships between words \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\]. For entities, the construction process is similar to that for words, though entity extraction methods are often required to first identify entities \[[303](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib303), [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib6), [317](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib317)\].

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i2.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Word-Sentence edge:</span> Word-sentence edges connect words to sentences based primarily on the relationship of belonging. These edges are constructed by linking words to the sentences they appear in \[[408](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib408), [155](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib155), [63](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib63)\], with edge weights often measured using term frequency-inverse document frequency (TF-IDF). Besides, Ramesh et al. \[[332](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib332)\] connects the entities with passage titles via hyperlinks with existing out-of-box entity linkers.

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i3.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Sentence-Sentence edge:</span> Sentence-sentence edges connect sentences based on their semantic similarity or relationships. For example, these edges can be constructed through sentence interactions \[[491](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib491), [284](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib284), [165](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib165)\], similarities between TF-IDF representations \[[237](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib237), [45](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib45)\], BM25 \[[297](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib297)\], sentence embeddings \[[260](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib260), [410](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib410), [525](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib525)\], Part of speech (Pos) feature and N-Gram feature \[[482](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib482)\]. Additionally, Zheng and Kordjamshidi \[[556](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib556)\] construct a Semantic Role Labeling(SRL) graph using AllenNLP-SRL model \[[359](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib359)\]. This allows for connecting sentences within long documents or across different documents, which is particularly useful for LLMs with limited context length.

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i4.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Sentence-document edge:</span> Sentence-document edges are constructed by linking sentences to the specific documents they belong to \[[392](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib392)\].

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i5.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S4.I2.i2.I1.i5.p1.1.1">Document-document edge:</span> Document-document edges connect documents based on their similarities. These edges can be constructed when entities in one document are referenced or shared by another \[[392](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib392), [90](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib90)\], through document clustering \[[410](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib410)\], embedding similarity \[[237](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib237)\], topic similarity \[[525](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib525)\] or structural similarity \[[260](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib260)\].

Document graphs typically exhibit heterogeneity, consisting of multiple types of edges. In addition, several hierarchical graphs have been proposed \[[444](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib444), [540](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib540)\], where different levels of abstraction are captured. The graph structure can also be dynamic or updated during the learning process \[[408](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib408), [302](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib302), [410](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib410), [259](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib259)\]. Moreover, the node in the document graph can also be the response of LLMs. For example, GoR \[[520](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib520)\] connects the history responses of LLMs with the responding chunks of documents for long-context summarization.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.3 </span> Retriever

The retrievers for document graphs typically follow the general retriever design, as described in Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2.4</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.SS4 "2.4 Retriever 𝛀^\"Retriever\" ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)"). However, there are some special retrieval methods as follows:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I3.i1.p1.1.1">Pre-Retrieval:</span> As introduced earlier, there are many scenarios where building a document graph is necessary. However, constructing a fine-grained graph for a large volume of documents can be inefficient and unnecessary. The pre-retrieval aims to first retrieve relevant documents based on the query and then construct a graph. For example, Thayaparan et al. \[[392](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib392)\] use pre-trained GloVe vectors to first extract relevant sentences and then construct a graph based on the retrieved sentences. Zheng and Kordjamshidi \[[556](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib556)\], Yu et al. \[[498](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib498)\] also construct graphs based on the retrieved information.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I3.i2.p1.1.1">Graph similarity-based retriever:</span> Graph similarity aims to measure the similarity between two graphs. If both the query and the retrieval data source are graphs, calculating the graph similarity is essential to retrieve relevant information. For instance, \[[544](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib544)\] leverages the General Maximum Common Subgraph (GMCS) method to retrieve related graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I3.i3.p1.1.1">Iterative Retriever:</span> In certain tasks, such as multi-step question answering, the node containing the answer might not be directly similar to the query. Iterative retrievers address this by first retrieving nodes related to the query and then using the retrieved information to iteratively retrieve subsequent nodes. For example, Wang et al. \[[428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428)\], Zhang et al. \[[541](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib541)\] utilize iterative retrieval methods for multi-step question-answering tasks, and Ma et al. \[[278](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib278)\] iteratively retrieve the documents based on the existing knowledge graph. Asai et al. \[[10](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib10)\] train a Recurrent Neural Network (RNN) to recurrently retrieve relevant information with the Bayesian Personalized Ranking (BRR) loss.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I3.i4.p1.1.1">Topology-based Retriever:</span> Various topological relationships in a graph can be used to measure different types of similarity. For example, proximity-based topological similarity measures the structural distance between two nodes, while role-based topological similarity assesses the similarity of the roles nodes play within the graph. These similarities can also be leveraged in the retrieval process \[[429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\].

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.4 </span> Organizer

In this section, we describe the organizers for document graphs. GraphRAG on document graphs is still in its early stage, and many works do not incorporate explicit organizers, as shown in Section [<span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2.5</span>](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#S2.SS5 "2.5 Organizer ‣ 2 A Holistic Framework of GraphRAG ‣ Retrieval-Augmented Generation with Graphs (GraphRAG)"). We summarize the existing methods below:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I4.i1.p1.1.1">Graph Pruning</span>: Graph Pruning refines the retrieved subgraph to reduce irrelevant information and improve computational efficiency. For example, Hemmati and Ghassem-Sani \[[149](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib149)\] prune graphs based on the local clustering coefficient, while Zhang et al. \[[539](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib539)\] employs path-centric pruning to incorporate off-path information. Li et al. \[[229](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib229)\] dynamically drop irrelevant nodes during decoding, and Angelova and Weikum \[[8](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib8)\] prune edges based on a similarity threshold. Additionally, Edge et al. \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\] uses community detection algorithms to create distinct communities that are then fed into the generators.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I4.i2.p1.1.1">Reranking</span>: Reranking methods aim to reorder retrieved information to facilitate the generation. For example, Yu et al. \[[498](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib498)\], Zhang et al. \[[541](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib541)\], and Dong et al. \[[90](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib90)\] use GNNs to rerank retrieved passages. Li et al. \[[239](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib239)\] perform listwise reranking to reorder passages expanded via the graph structure.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.5 </span> Generator

In this section, we summarize commonly used generators for document graphs. Various methods are applied within document graphs, depending on the input format. Some approaches use the entire graph as input, in which case GNNs and Graph Transformers are commonly employed. Other methods take individual sentences as input, where RNNs or (Large) Language Models are suitable. Additionally, some works leverage both graph and text as inputs, requiring integrated methods that can process multimodal data effectively. We detail each category in the following:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I5.i1.p1.1.1">GNN-based generator</span>: Numerous works model tasks as graph-related problems, leveraging GNNs as generators. Conventional GNNs, such as GCN \[[206](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib206)\], GraphSAGE \[[138](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib138)\], and GAT \[[402](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib402)\], are widely adopted in various studies \[[320](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib320), [392](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib392), [491](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib491), [300](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib300), [313](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib313), [90](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib90)\]. When graphs contain edge relations, Relational Graph Convolutional Networks (R-GCNs) are often used to capture these relationships effectively \[[76](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib76), [297](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib297)\]. In addition, some works incorporate graph contrastive learning techniques to enhance performance \[[462](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib462), [524](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib524), [149](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib149)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I5.i2.p1.1.1">Graph Transformer-based generator</span>: Graph transformers \[[504](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib504)\], which capture global information across the graph, are used to encode graph structures for various tasks \[[518](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib518), [292](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib292)\]. These models leverage the transformer’s self-attention mechanism to capture dependencies beyond local neighborhoods, making them well-suited for tasks requiring global context.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I5.i3.p1.1.1">RNN-based generator</span>: Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs), such as LSTMs, are popular for processing sequences. Therefore, RNNs are employed when the input is in text form \[[369](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib369)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I5.i4.p1.1.1">LLM-based generator</span>: LLM-based generators typically transform the retrieved subgraph into text before using large language models (LLMs) for generation \[[428](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib428), [98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\]. Various models are used in this approach. For instance, BERT \[[81](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib81)\] has been applied by \[[401](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib401), [233](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib233), [541](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib541)\] to support generation tasks, while Yu et al. \[[498](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib498)\] and Ju et al. \[[191](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib191)\] leverage the T5 model \[[329](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib329)\], and Chen et al. \[[48](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib48)\] use RoBERTa \[[263](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib263)\]

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I5.i5.p1.1.1">Integrated Generator</span>: Some works leverage both graph and text data simultaneously for generation, using integrated generators that combine graph models with text generation models. For example, Xu et al. \[[476](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib476)\] and Wang et al. \[[410](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib410)\] employ a combination of RoBERTa and GCN, while Ramesh et al. \[[332](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib332)\] fuse GNN with T5 to harness the strengths of both graph structure and language model capabilities.

In addition to the aforementioned generators, some approaches embed graphs directly into text generation models. For example, Li et al. \[[237](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib237)\] proposes replacing traditional self-attention layers in transformers with graph-informed self-attention, enabling the model to integrate graph structure directly into the generation process.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.6 </span> Resources and Tools

The data resources for document GraphRAG can include any type of document, and thus, we do not provide a comprehensive list here. Instead, in the following, we introduce several tools specifically designed or commonly used for GraphRAG on document graphs:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i1.p1.1.1">CoreNLP: <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote8"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">8</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">8</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote8.1.1.1">8</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter ltx_font_medium" href="https://github.com/stanfordnlp/CoreNLP" title="">https://github.com/stanfordnlp/CoreNLP</a></span></span></span></span> The Stanford CoreNLP natural language processing toolkit offers a comprehensive set of natural language processing tools, including token and sentence boundaries, parts of speech, named entities, numeric and time values, dependency and constituency parses, coreference, sentiment, quote attributions, and relations. These tools are valuable for constructing document graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i2.p1.1.1">spaCy: <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote9"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">9</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">9</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote9.1.1.1">9</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter ltx_font_medium" href="https://github.com/explosion/spaCy" title="">https://github.com/explosion/spaCy</a></span></span></span></span> The spaCy is an advanced natural language processing library known for its speed and neural network models, which are optimized for tasks such as tagging, parsing, named entity recognition, text classification, part-of-speech tagging, dependency parsing, sentence segmentation, lemmatization, morphological analysis, and entity linking.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i3.p1.1.1">BLINK</span>: <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote10"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">10</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">10</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">10</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter" href="https://github.com/facebookresearch/BLINK" title="">https://github.com/facebookresearch/BLINK</a></span></span></span> BLINK is an entity linking python library that uses Wikipedia as the target knowledge base.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i4.p1.1.1">OpenIE<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote11"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">11</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">11</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote11.1.1.1">11</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter ltx_font_medium" href="https://github.com/dair-iitd/OpenIE-standalone" title="">https://github.com/dair-iitd/OpenIE-standalone</a></span></span></span></span>: Open Information Extraction (OpenIE) is a tool for extracting structured information from text. It is valuable for generating triples from unstructured text, which can be used as nodes and edges in document graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i5.p1.1.1">CogComp NLP<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote12"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">12</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">12</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote12.1.1.1">12</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter ltx_font_medium" href="https://github.com/CogComp/cogcomp-nlp" title="">https://github.com/CogComp/cogcomp-nlp</a></span></span></span></span>: Developed by the Cognitive Computation Group, this suite includes tools for natural language processing tasks such as named entity recognition, sentiment analysis, and coreference resolution.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i6.p1.1.1">GraphRAG</span> \[[98](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib98)\]<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote13"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">13</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">13</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">13</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter" href="https://github.com/microsoft/graphrag" title="">https://github.com/microsoft/graphrag</a></span></span></span>: GraphRAG is a data pipeline and transformation suite that is designed to extract meaningful, structured data from unstructured text using the power of LLMs. The process involves extracting a knowledge graph out of raw text, building a community hierarchy, generating summaries for these communities, and then leveraging these structures when performing RAG-based tasks.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i7.p1.1.1">LangChain</span> <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote14"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">14</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">14</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">14</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter" href="https://www.langchain.com/" title="">https://www.langchain.com/</a></span></span></span>: LangChain is a framework for developing applications powered by LLMs, with use cases in document analysis and summarization, RAG, chatbots, and code analysis. Notably, LangChain supports Graph Transformers, which convert documents into graph-structured formats, making it highly suitable for processing document graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i8.p1.1.1">Neo4j<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote15"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">15</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">15</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote15.1.1.1">15</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter ltx_font_medium" href="https://neo4j.com/" title="">https://neo4j.com/</a></span></span></span></span>: Neo4j is a graph database platform offering comprehensive tools for storing, visualizing, managing, and querying graph data. It includes an LLM Graph Builder, which can extract graphs with LLMs. It also provides GraphRAG demos to demonstrate how to implement a LLMs and RAG system with Neo4j.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i9.p1.1.1">LlamaIndex</span> \[[253](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib253)\]<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote16"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">16</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">16</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">16</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter" href="https://www.llamaindex.ai/" title="">https://www.llamaindex.ai/</a></span></span></span>: LlamaIndex is a data framework designed to support the development of applications based on LLMs. It allows developers to seamlessly integrate data sources, ranging from various file formats to applications and databases, with LLMs. LlamaIndex features an efficient data retrieval and query interface, enabling developers to input any LLM prompt and receive context-rich, knowledge-enhanced outputs. Notably, it includes a Property Graph Index that facilitates the building, modeling, storage, and querying of graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S4.I6.i10.p1.1.1">Haystack<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote17"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">17</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">17</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote17.1.1.1">17</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_url ltx_font_typewriter ltx_font_medium" href="https://haystack.deepset.ai/" title="">https://haystack.deepset.ai/</a></span></span></span></span>: Haystack is an end-to-end framework for building applications powered by LLMs, Transformer models, vector search, and more. It supports a variety of use cases, including RAG, document search, question answering, and answer generation. Haystack enables the orchestration of state-of-the-art embedding models and LLMs into custom pipelines for creating comprehensive NLP applications. Additionally, it supports Neo4j as a DocumentStore, making it suitable for document graph storage and query operations.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">5 </span> Scientific Graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_sci.png)

Scientific graphs refer to graph-structured data used in domains such as drug discovery \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433), [167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167), [265](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib265)\] and biomedicine \[[484](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib484), [255](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib255), [78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78), [185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185), [30](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib30), [443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443)\], both of which are common application areas for GraphRAG. Therefore, in this section, scientific graphs specifically refer to molecular graphs and medical graphs.

In recent years, significant progress has been made in the development of artificial intelligence for science \[[1](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib1), [193](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib193), [393](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib393), [283](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib283)\]. Machine learning (ML) and deep neural network technologies are increasingly driving scientific discovery from experimental data. Notably, generative models such as Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved remarkable success in working with scientific graph data, including molecular graphs and biomedical graphs. In molecular graphs, for example, atoms serve as nodes, while chemical bonds represent edges, capturing the structure of molecules. AI technologies can handle both prediction and generation tasks on these graphs, driving advancements in fields like drug discovery.

Despite the impressive capabilities of generative models like LLMs, several challenges still hinder their applications to scientific domains. One of the most prominent challenges is the lack of domain-specific expertise. In fields like drug discovery, molecule generation is crucial, yet traditional generative models often struggle with producing incorrect or scientifically invalid structures. In medical question-answering (QA) tasks, incorrect answers, hallucinations, and limited interpretability are frequently encountered. To address these challenges, recent works have proposed leveraging external knowledge databases to enhance generation via GraphRAG. GraphRAG improves accuracy by retrieving relevant scientific graphs from extensive databases to guide the generation or answering process. This approach ensures scientific validity by incorporating known valid graph structures, leverages existing knowledge for practical applications, and accelerates the generation process by narrowing the search space.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.1 </span> Application Tasks
