Scientific graphs are beneficial for a wide range of tasks. In this subsection, we will review some representative tasks where scientific graphs can play a significant role.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Molecule generation:</span> Molecule generation refers to the process of creating or designing new molecular structures, often using generative models \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433), [167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\]. It plays a crucial role in fields like drug discovery. The use of scientific graphs, especially molecular graphs, can enhance the rationality and accuracy of generated molecular structures in molecular generation. Typically, an inquiry such as a molecule is given to retrieve the most relevant molecular structures to guide molecular generation.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Molecule property prediction:</span> Molecule property prediction refers to the use of computational methods to estimate the physical, chemical, or biological properties of molecules based on their structure \[[265](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib265)\]. It has proven highly effective in accelerating the drug discovery process while significantly reducing associated costs. The use of scientific graphs, especially molecular graphs, can enhance the accuracy of prediction. To achieve this, a query molecule is provided, and similar molecules are identified as demonstrations to improve the prediction.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Question answering:</span> Question answering (QA) in the scientific domain refers to the use of computational methods to provide accurate and context-specific answers to scientific questions \[[484](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib484), [185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185), [443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443), [225](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib225), [175](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib175), [449](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib449), [366](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib366)\]. This involves retrieving or generating information from scientific literature, databases, or other resources to address complex, domain-specific queries, such as "After meals, I feel a bit of stomach reflux. What medication should I take for it?" In graghRAG, the scientific literature is usually converted into a knowledge graph to provide a basis for answering questions or to enrich the query.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.2 </span> Scientific Graph Construction

In GraphRAG, the choice and construction of data sources are crucial and typically include both public and private datasets. Public datasets are often derived from widely recognized resources, such as molecular databases like PubChem \[[204](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib204)\], ChEMBL \[[121](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib121)\], and ZINC \[[170](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib170)\], as well as biomedical literature and data sources like PubMed \[[269](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib269)\] and ClinicalTrials \[[509](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib509)\]. These datasets provide a broad foundation of information across various domains, offering reliable and authoritative references for the model. On the other hand, private datasets contain user-specific information, such as medical records from hospitals or confidential clinical trial data \[[449](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib449)\]. These datasets are highly confidential and unique, enabling GraphRAG models to offer personalized and proprietary knowledge support.

For chemistry, molecules can be represented in four main forms: 1D SMILES (Simplified Molecular Input Line Entry System) \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433), [265](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib265)\], 2D molecular graphs \[[274](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib274)\], 3D molecular graphs with coordinates \[[167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\], and text captions describing molecular structures \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\]. 1D SMILES is a linear string generated through depth-first search (DFS) on the molecular graph, following specific rules. 2D molecular graphs represent atoms as nodes and bonds as edges, visually showing the connectivity of atoms. 3D molecular graphs incorporate spatial coordinates for each atom, reflecting the molecule’s structure in three-dimensional space, which is crucial for tasks such as molecular docking and reaction prediction. Text captions, on the other hand, provide a natural language description of the molecular structure, which can be used in tasks that involve textual data interpreting chemical structures from text. Scientific graphs can typically be constructed as follows:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i1.p1.1.1">Text-based construction</span>: Text-based graph construction is the most commonly used method, which can transform textual scientific knowledge into knowledge graphs. Delile et al. \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\] believe that text captions suffer from issues of information redundancy and imbalance. By constructing text as a knowledge graph, it is possible to rebalance the retrievable information and reduce redundancy. Building a knowledge graph typically involves two key steps:

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i1.I1.i1.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I2.i1.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Entity Extraction:</span> This step involves identifying and extracting key entities from the text, such as domain-specific terms (e.g., chemical compounds, genes).

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i1.I1.i2.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I2.i1.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Relationship Extraction:</span> After identifying the entities, the next step is to extract the relationships between these entities, determining how they are linked in the given context (e.g., "A is related to B"). This step forms the structural backbone of the knowledge graph.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i2.p1.1.1">SMILES-based construction</span>: Many chemical databases store data in analytical descriptor formats such as SMILES  \[[434](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib434)\]. To model graphs, SMILES representations need to be transformed into graph structures. SMILES-based construction utilizes a library such as RDKit \[[217](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib217)\] to read the SMILES notation, create a molecular object, and extract atomic and bonding information to construct a 2D graph. Each atom in the molecule is represented as a node, and each bond is represented as an edge between nodes. The atoms carry features such as atom type, degree, charge, and aromaticity, while the bonds can include bond type (single, double, etc.) and whether they are part of a ring.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I2.i3.p1.1.1">3D graph construction</span>: Huang et al. \[[167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\] introduce IRDIFF, an interaction-based retrieval-augmented 3D molecular diffusion model designed for target-specific molecule generation. For pretraining, PMINet is utilized to capture interactive structural context information with binding affinity signals, leveraging the PDBbind v2016 dataset. This dataset offers 3D protein structures and a substantial set of experimentally validated protein-ligand complexes. These protein structures are primarily obtained via techniques like X-ray crystallography, nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), or cryo-electron microscopy, containing detailed atomic coordinates, bond angles, and secondary structure information.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.3 </span> Retriever

The retriever is responsible for locating relevant information based on the input query. In GraphRAG, this information typically consists of graph-structured data. The retriever can be roughly classified as heuristic-based retriever and deep learning-based retriever.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I3.i1.p1.1.1">Heuristic-based retriever:</span> A heuristic-based retriever employs predefined rules, algorithms, and heuristics to identify and retrieve relevant knowledge from graph-structured data sources. Heuristic-based retrieval can be categorized into several types as below:

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i1.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Similarity-based retriever:</span> Wang et al. \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433)\] retrieve exemplar molecules based on their similarity to the input molecule, using cosine similarity as the metric. MindMap \[[443](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib443)\] encodes the entities from the query and the external knowledge graph into dense embeddings using BERT, and then retrieves the entity set with the highest similarity scores.

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i2.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Matching-based retriever:</span> A matching-based method provides an alternative approach, enabling LLMs to generate evidence-based responses by leveraging comprehensive private data. Specifically, Wu et al. \[[449](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib449)\] identify the most relevant graph through a top-down matching process. Once the relevant content is identified, the LLM generates an intermediate response with its assistance, enhancing both the transparency and interpretability of the results.

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i3.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Knowledge graph-based retriever:</span> A knowledge graph-based method can effectively identify and retrieve the necessary information to respond to a query. Pelletier et al. \[[318](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib318)\], Li et al. \[[225](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib225)\] use named entity recognition and relation extraction to connect user queries with relevant entities in the knowledge graph, thereby uncovering interpretable and actionable insights from existing biomedical knowledge. This approach significantly enhances the transparency and utility of predictive models. Delile et al. \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\] map the text chunks to the knowledge graph, then utilize graph distances to find the chunks most relevant to the user’s question. In addition, this work introduces a scoring metric that balances the data by giving each concept mapped along the shortest path an equal opportunity. This metric prioritizes text chunks based on both their recency and their impact. HyKGE \[[185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185)\] first queries the LLM to generate a hypothetical output and extracts entities from both the output and the query. Then, HyKGE retrieves reasoning chains between any two anchor entities in an existing knowledge graph, such as CMeKG \[[30](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib30)\] and CPubMed-KG, and feeds the reasoning chains along with the query into the LLM. KG-Rank \[[484](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib484)\] identifies entities within the query and retrieves the related triples or sub-graphs from the KG to gather factual information.

    *   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i4.1.1.1">–</span></span>

        <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S5.I3.i1.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Fusion-based retriever:</span> Soman et al. \[[366](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib366)\] begins by retrieving the relevant node in the knowledge graph based on vector similarity to the query’s entity. Then, it retrieves the context triples (Subject, Predicate, Object) linked to this node within the knowledge graph.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I3.i2.p1.1.1">Deep Learning-based Retriever:</span> Deep learning-based retrievers can extract relevant knowledge to guide the generation process, such as the generation of molecules or proteins. Specifically, Huang et al. \[[167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\] uses a pre-trained protein-molecule interaction network named PMINet to extract interactive structural context information between the target protein and ligands in the reference pool to guide the generation of target-aware ligands. Jeong et al. \[[175](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib175)\] utilize the off-the-shelf MedCPT retriever, a tool specifically tailored for retrieving documents in response to biomedical queries, capable of retrieving up to ten relevant pieces of evidence for each input. DALK \[[225](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib225)\] filters out noise and retrieves the most relevant knowledge by utilizing the ranking capabilities of LLMs.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.4 </span> Organizer

Basically, there are two types of the organizer: embedding-based and query-based organizers according to how the retrieved knowledge is utilized.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I4.i1.p1.1.1">Query-based organizer.</span> The simplest and most direct organizer is a query-based organizer, which integrates the retrieved information with input queries to generate responses. Specifically, HyKGE \[[185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185)\] enhances the reasoning process by incorporating external knowledge into the large language model. In this framework, the retrieved reasoning chains are fed into the LLM alongside the original query. This enables the model to ground its responses in structured, contextually relevant information, improving both the accuracy and depth of the generated output. Wu et al. \[[449](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib449)\] prompt the LLM to answer the question by providing the retrieved entity names and their relationships in a concatenated form. This approach involves retrieving relevant entities and the relationships between them from an external knowledge source and structuring this information as a cohesive input for the LLM. DALK \[[225](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib225)\] integrates the query and retrieves knowledge and then feeds it into LLMs for reasoning and getting the predicted answer. Similarly, KG-Rank \[[484](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib484)\] combines the re-ranked triplets with the task prompt and inputs them into LLMs for answer generation. In contrast to the methods mentioned above, Soman et al. \[[366](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib366)\] begin with context pruning. Specifically, this approach refines the retrieved context by selecting only the most semantically relevant elements needed to answer the query promptly. The input prompt is then combined with this pruning-aware information, producing an enriched prompt that is fed into the LLM for text generation. Delile et al. \[[78](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib78)\] develop a data rebalancing mechanism to ensure that each entity relevant to a question has an equal opportunity of being represented while also highlighting recent significant discoveries. This rebalanced knowledge is then combined with the query prompt and provided as input to the LLM.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I4.i2.p1.1.1">Embeding-based organizer.</span> Embedding-based organizer integrates the retrieved information with input embedding to generate responses. Specifically, Wang et al. \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433)\] uses a lightweight, trainable standard cross-attention mechanism to fuse the embedding of the input molecule and the retrieved example molecules. Similarly, Huang et al. \[[167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\] uses a trainable cross-attention mechanism to fuse the enhanced embeddings of the retrieved example ligands and the generated molecules.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.5 </span> Generator

The generator is a core component of the GraphRAG model and is responsible for producing the final output by integrating retrieved evidence with the input data. Generators can be broadly classified into three categories according to the type of generated models they use: transformer-based generators, diffusion model-based generators, and large language models-based generators.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I5.i1.p1.1.1">Transformer-based generator:</span> RetMol \[[433](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib433)\] utilizes the Megatron version of the molecule generative model Chemformer for drug discovery. Specifically, the Chemformer model is a Transformer-based model that can be efficiently applied to tasks in chemistry.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I5.i2.p1.1.1">Diffusion-based generator:</span> Huang et al. \[[167](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib167)\] introduce a novel interaction-based retrieval-augmented diffusion model (IRDIFF) for structure-based drug design. Specifically, IRDIFF is able to generate molecules that bind strongly to the target pocket by utilizing protein-molecule interaction data between reference proteins and the target protein to guide the diffusion model.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I5.i3.p1.1.1">LLM-based generator:</span> Some approaches leverage large language models, such as LLaMA2, LLaMA3, GPT-4, and Gemini, etc \[[449](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib449), [175](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib175), [318](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib318), [265](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib265), [225](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib225), [366](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib366)\]. For instance, MedGraphRAG \[[449](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib449)\] utilizes GraphRAG to improve the answering capabilities of models such as LLaMA2, LLaMA3, Gemini, and GPT-4 for medical question answering. MolecularGPT \[[265](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib265)\] utilizes GraphRAG to improve GPT-3’s predictive capabilities for molecular property prediction. DALK \[[225](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib225)\] employs GraphRAG to boost GPT-3.5-turbo’s ability to answer questions related to Alzheimer’s Disease. HyKGE \[[185](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib185)\] utilizes GraphRAG to enhance the medical question-answering capabilities of GPT-3.5 and Baichuan13B.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">5.6 </span> Resources and Tools

In this section, we provide an overview of common data sources and tools utilized in graph RAG systems within the scientific domain, along with a brief description of each project.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">5.6.1 </span> Data Resources

The public datasets are commonly from well-known resources, such as molecular databases:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I6.i1.p1.1.1">PubChem</span> \[[204](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib204)\]: PubChem encompasses a wide range of chemical data, including 2D and 3D structures, chemical and physical properties, bioactivity, pharmacology, toxicology, drug targets, metabolism, safety guidelines, associated patents, and scientific literature. Most of its entries pertain to small molecules, with a primary emphasis on those containing fewer than 100 atoms and 1,000 bonds.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I6.i2.p1.1.1">ChEMBL</span> \[[121](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib121)\]: It is an openly accessible database containing detailed information on drugs, drug-like small molecules, and their bioactivity. This curated resource stands out for its comprehensive coverage of the drug discovery process, encompassing data on more than 2.2 million compounds and over 18 million records documenting their effects on biological systems. ChEMBL provides insights into the interactions between small molecules and their protein targets, along with data on how these compounds influence cellular and organismal functions. It also includes information on ADMET (absorption, distribution, metabolism, excretion, and toxicity) profiles. The database stores two-dimensional structures, calculated molecular properties (such as logP, molecular weight, and Lipinski’s Rule of Five parameters), and bioactivity data like binding affinities and pharmacological effects.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I6.i3.p1.1.1">ZINC</span> \[[170](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib170)\]: The ZINC dataset is a curated collection of commercially available chemical compounds designed specifically for virtual screening purposes. It offers over 230 million purchasable compounds in 3D formats that are ready for docking, as well as more than 750 million compounds available for analog searches. Each molecule is adjusted to biologically relevant protonation states and is annotated with properties such as molecular weight, calculated LogP, and rotatable bonds. The library includes vendor and purchasing details, making it compatible with several widely used docking software programs. Compounds are provided in multiple protonation states and tautomeric forms within certain constraints, and some formats even offer multiple conformations per molecule. The ZINC database is available for free download <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote18"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">18</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">18</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">18</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="http://zinc.docking.org" title="">http://zinc.docking.org</a></span></span></span> in multiple common file formats, including SMILES, mol2, 3D SDF, and DOCK flexibase.

There are also biomedical data sources as below:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I7.i1.p1.1.1">PubMed</span> \[[269](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib269)\]: PubMed is a freely accessible database that primarily houses the MEDLINE collection, containing references and abstracts in life sciences and biomedicine. Managed by the United States National Library of Medicine (NLM) within the National Institutes of Health, PubMed is part of the Entrez retrieval system. As of May 23, 2023, PubMed contains over 35 million citations and abstracts, with records dating back to 1966 and selectively to 1865, and a few even to 1809. On this date, approximately 24.6 million records include abstracts, and 26.8 million provide links to full-text articles, with around 10.9 million available freely. In the last decade (up to December 31, 2019), PubMed added nearly one million new records annually on average.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I7.i2.p1.1.1">ClinicalTrials</span> \[[509](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib509)\]: ClinicalTrials is a global registry and database that provides information on clinical studies funded by both private and public sources. Managed by the U.S. National Library of Medicine, this resource includes a summary of each study’s protocol, and for some studies, results are available in a tabular format. Users can search studies by criteria such as study status, condition or disease, country, and other keywords. Continuously updated, the database now includes over 300,000 research studies conducted across all U.S. states and in more than 200 countries worldwide.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I7.i3.p1.1.1">Open-source medical KGs</span>: CMeKG (Clinical Medicine Knowledge Graph) <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote19"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">19</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">19</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">19</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://cmekg.pcl.ac.cn/" title="">https://cmekg.pcl.ac.cn/</a></span></span></span>, CPubMed-KG (Large-scale Chinese Open Medical Knowledge Graph) <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote20"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">20</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">20</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">20</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://cpubmed.openi.org.cn/graph/wiki" title="">https://cpubmed.openi.org.cn/graph/wiki</a></span></span></span> and Disease-KG (Chinese disease Knowledge Graph) <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote21"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">21</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">21</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">21</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://github.com/nuolade/disease-kb" title="">https://github.com/nuolade/disease-kb</a></span></span></span> are open-source medical knowledge graphs that consolidate a vast amount of medical text data, covering areas such as diseases, medications, symptoms, and diagnostic treatments. The combined knowledge graph includes 1,288,721 entities and 3,569,427 relations.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">5.6.2 </span> Tools

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I8.i1.p1.1.1">RDKit</span> \[[217](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib217)\]: RDKit is open-source toolkit for cheminformatics. RDKit has several key features: it can process chemical structures by reading and writing various file formats, such as SMILES, InChI, and Mol files. It generates multiple types of molecular fingerprints, enabling chemical structure comparison and similarity searches. RDKit also provides algorithms for calculating molecular similarity and supports the representation and processing of chemical reactions, including the identification of reactants and products. Although it does not offer molecular docking capabilities on its own, RDKit can be integrated with other docking tools. Additionally, RDKit supports machine learning algorithms, allowing for pattern recognition in chemical data and the construction of predictive models.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S5.I8.i2.p1.1.1">CADRO</span>: The Common Alzheimer’s and Related Dementias Research Ontology (CADRO) <span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote22"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">22</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">22</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note">22</span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href" href="https://iadrp.nia.nih.gov/about/cadro" title="">https://iadrp.nia.nih.gov/about/cadro</a></span></span></span> is employed to extract a subset of Alzheimer’s disease (AD)-related samples from the medical QA datasets for evaluation. CADRO organizes terms into a three-tiered classification system with eight main categories and multiple subcategories focused on AD and related dementias, containing frequently used terminologies or keywords in the field. From CADRO, users obtain a list of AD-related keywords most relevant to the medical QA datasets.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">6 </span> Social Graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_soc.png)

The social graph typically consists of entities connected by their social relations and is ubiquitous across real-world applications. A primary example is social networks like Twitter and Facebook, where entities represent individuals linked by social interactions (e.g., friendships, followers/followees, likes, and mentions). These social graphs extend beyond human interactions and are not confined to living entities, such as tortoises co-using the same burrow in animal social networks \[[340](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib340)\], complementary products co-purchased by the same customer in E-commerce recommender systems \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427), [452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\], or even the LLM-simulated social agents \[[523](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib523), [241](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib241)\]. The wealth of social-relational knowledge in these social graphs is the golden resource for GraphRAG\[[511](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib511), [183](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib183), [463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463), [429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510), [427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427), [77](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib77), [95](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib95), [452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452), [159](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib159), [528](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib528), [327](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib327), [438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438), [202](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib202), [396](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib396)\] , which is reviewed in this section.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">6.1 </span> Application Tasks

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I1.i1.p1.1.1">Entity Property Prediction</span>: Entity property prediction focuses on predicting properties and classifying categories for social entities in social networks, examples of which include the prediction of partnership compatibility, assessment of morality, detection of account suspensions, identification of toxic behaviors \[[183](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib183)\] and product property prediction \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Text Generation</span>: Text generation aims to produce text that aligns with social contexts and norms. Typically, the interplay between structural and textual information, such as proximity-based network homophily and role-based similarity \[[5](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib5)\], serves as the foundation for text generation. For instance, Wang et al. \[[429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\] retrieves the texts of proximity/role-similar nodes to enhance the text generation of the target node. Kim et al. \[[202](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib202)\], Xie et al. \[[463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463)\] generate personalized recommendation explanations by retrieving customers’/products’ historical reviews. In addition, some other works also leverage semantic similarity to retrieve reference/attributes/opinions and augment the downstream review generation \[[353](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib353), [92](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib92), [315](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib315)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Recommendation</span>: The recommendation task aims to find the most relevant items to satisfy user demands. Due to the missing and sparse customer/item interactions (e.g., cold-start issues), GraphRAG can be naturally applied to boost customer/item sparse interaction by retrieving additional meta-knowledge. Depending on the concrete recommendation scenario, GraphRAG has been used for graph-based recommendation \[[438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438), [95](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib95)\], next-item recommendation \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427), [159](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib159), [528](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib528), [327](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib327), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510), [416](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib416)\], and conversational recommendation \[[116](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib116)\]

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Question-answering</span>: Question-answering tasks are encountered not only in knowledge and document graph domains but also in social graphs. For example, a user might ask, "What are the best parks for family gatherings around Los Gatos?" and expect a personalized query answer \[[511](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib511), [198](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib198)\]. Furthermore, such questions might explicitly require graph-structured reasoning. For instance, Wu et al. \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\] build a semi-structured knowledge base, and some queries might ask, "Can you list the products made by Nike?" Answering this question requires not only a deep understanding of the query but also familiarity with the structural information of the data.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I1.i5.p1.1.1">Fake News Detection</span>: Detecting fake news necessitates considering both semantic content (e.g., the content of the news) and structural interactions (e.g., interactions among news). For instance, Ram et al. \[[331](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib331)\] evaluates the credibility of a Reddit post based on the credibility of other Reddit posts retrieved by their common interactions with the same common commenters.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">6.2 </span> Social Graph Construction

The relations that GraphRAG leverages to derive additional information from social graphs can be mainly summarized into three rationales: proximity-based, role-based, and personalization-based rationale. The proximity-based rationale, rooted in the adage "birds of a feather flock together," suggests that nodes close to each other in a social network often share similar properties \[[291](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib291)\]. For example, close friends tend to possess similar hobbies. The role-based rationale focuses on nodes with similar local subgraph structures sharing similar features or label distributions \[[93](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib93)\]. For example, managers at the same hierarchical level within a company generally have comparable job titles and responsibilities, and hub airports exhibit similar operational characteristics and strategic importance. Lastly, the personalization-based rationale refers to individuals’ uniqueness regarding their characteristics and interactions. For example, in recommender systems, users interact with items in various ways, such as clicking, viewing, adding to a cart, purchasing, and reviewing, each interaction providing valuable knowledge that GraphRAG can leverage to personalize generated content. Based on the above three relational rationales in the social graphs, the social graph construction methods can be summarized as follows:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I2.i1.p1.1.1">User-User-Interaction</span> \[[183](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib183)\]: This type of social graph represents user-to-user interactions commonly seen on social networks such as Twitter, Reddit, and Facebook. Examples include follower-followee relationships on Twitter, friendship relationships on Facebook, and user comments on other users’ threads on Reddit. Note that this user-user interaction naturally exists in the real world without human curation or modification compared to documents or knowledge graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I2.i2.p1.1.1">User-Item-Interaction</span> \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427), [429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429), [463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463), [202](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib202)\]: This type of social graph represents user-to-item interactions commonly found on e-commerce platforms like Amazon and eBay. These interactions, including purchasing, adding-to-the-cart, and viewing, can be modeled as a bipartite graph, where each type of interaction reflects a unique user intention.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I2.i3.p1.1.1">Item-Item-Interaction</span> \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427), [528](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib528), [452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\]: This social graph captures interactions between items, typically identified by shared interactions from the same customer or user. For example, a "co-view" interaction between two products indicates that they are viewed by the same customer, while a "view-add-to-cart" interaction indicates that one product is first viewed and the other is added to the cart next by the same customer. In e-commerce networks, these co-interactions between two items can be broadly categorized into complementary and substitute relationships \[[554](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib554)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I2.i4.p1.1.1">Metadata-Interaction</span> \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452), [485](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib485)\]: Items and users often possess metadata. For example, products on platforms like Amazon may include brand, manufacturer, and color attributes. This metadata can be represented as additional node types and the corresponding edges indicating relations between products and attributes, such as ownership or association.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I2.i5.p1.1.1">Agent-Agent Interaction</span> \[[51](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib51), [523](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib523)\]: With the increasing intelligence of LLMs, recent literature has explored the potential of using LLM-powered agents to simulate social behaviors, such as collaboration, debate, and reflection. These agent-agent interactions could also be used for GraphRAG.

Note that among the five types of interaction mentioned above, the user-user, user-item, and metadata relations are naturally formulated without human curation. In contrast, item-item interactions are generated by manual extraction, and agent-agent interactions require simulations.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">6.3 </span> Retriever

The relations of the social graphs constructed according to the above methods can be leveraged in GraphRAG to enhance downstream tasks by retrieving additional information. For example, retrieving historical metadata and customer interactions can improve recommendations. Next, we review representative retrieval methods in GraphRAG for social graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I3.i1.p1.1.1">ID-based Retriever</span>: Similar to entity linking, the ID-based retriever works by retrieving content specifically generated by a user/item, examples of which include retrieving historical item interactions of a specific customer \[[511](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib511), [183](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib183), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510)\], reviews posted on a specific product \[[463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463)\], and meta-data information about a specific user \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452), [346](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib346)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I3.i2.p1.1.1">Filtering-based Retriever</span>: Based on the ID-based retriever, the Filtering-based Retriever retrieves additional content based on collaborative filtering \[[416](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib416)\]. For user filtering, Top K users are identified by comparing the similarity of their historical item interactions with those of the target user demanding recommendation. To enhance the context of the target user, it retrieves the most popular items from those Top K users. In contrast, for item filtering, it identifies the Top K items that are most similar to the current item by comparing their user-interaction history. Among them, the most popular ones would be fetched to augment the current item recommendation.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I3.i3.p1.1.1">Social Relational Retriever</span>: Like the ID-based Retriever, the Social Relational Retriever focuses on retrieving knowledge from entities sharing certain relations with the target entities on hand. For example, Du et al. \[[95](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib95)\] hierarchically retrieve neighboring texts several hops from the central node to augment the semantic information of the target user/item. Meanwhile, Wang et al. \[[429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\] retrieve texts corresponding to nodes that share high proximity-based and role-based similarity, respectively.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I3.i4.p1.1.1">Integrated Neural-Symbolic Retriever</span>: This approach leverages both symbolic and neural retrievers to improve retrieval effectiveness \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510), [409](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib409)\]. The symbolic retriever retrieves information by following explicitly defined rules, such as retrieving based on identifiers, structured relationships, or interaction patterns, ensuring that the retrieved data strictly aligns with specific criteria. Meanwhile, the neural retriever complements this by using embedding-based similarity, capturing nuanced patterns and contextual relationships that may not be directly encoded in rules. Integrating them together provides a better trade-off between rule-based precision and neural-based adaptability and generalizability. For instance, Wang et al. \[[409](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib409), [427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\], Huang et al. \[[159](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib159)\], Qiu et al. \[[327](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib327)\] first retrieve K-hop neighboring products from the product knowledge graph (symbolic retriever) for products in the user session and further utilize neural-based adaptive filtering to aggregate items that are most relevant to the current sequence (i.e., neural retriever). Similarly, Zeng et al. \[[510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510)\] address data sparsity and heterogeneity by combining ID-based retrieval that retrieves movies based on the user ID of one party with a text-based retriever that enables movie retrieval between parties.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">6.4 </span> Organizer

To further enhance the retrieved content, the organizer for the social graphs employs specialized techniques beyond the typical re-ranking and filtering used in other graph domains \[[511](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib511), [152](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib152), [77](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib77)\]. For social graphs, the organizer of GraphRAG often uses Keyword Extraction, Profile Summarization, and Hierarchical Graph Aggregation to curate the retrieved content:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I4.i1.p1.1.1">Keyword Extraction</span>: Keyword extraction identifies the most relevant and informative keywords from the retrieved content. These extracted keywords guide the downstream generator in prioritizing attention and reduce the risk of overwhelming LLMs with excessive context. For example, Xie et al. \[[463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463)\] use an embedding estimator to pinpoint keywords aligned with a personalized latent query, which are then used to generate explanations.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I4.i2.p1.1.1">Profile Summarization</span>: Profile summarization creates rich and detailed user profiles that capture attributes such as age, gender, preferred and disliked genres, favorite directors, country, and language derived from users’ past interactions and item metadata \[[438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438)\]. This enriched user data increases the informativeness of the retrieved content while safeguarding privacy by using rewritten profiles. Additionally, after retrieving related movies via user/item filtering, Wang and Lim \[[416](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib416)\] apply a three-step prompting strategy to extract features tailored to the user and select representative movies by directly instructing LLMs. These extracted features and representative movies are further used as the user profile to guide the final recommendation generation. Guo et al. \[[137](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib137)\] leverage LLMs to generate more details about the entity profile based on its keywords/short phrases from external data to aid text generation.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I4.i3.p1.1.1">Hierarchical Graph Aggregation and Summarization</span>: When retrieving neighborhood textual information, the exponentially expanding receptive field at higher neighborhood layers often causes the aggregated content to exceed manageable limits, potentially diminishing the LLM’s effectiveness \[[236](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib236)\]. This challenge highlights the need for hierarchical graph aggregation and summarization. The primary idea is to first summarize neighborhood information retrieved at each layer before propagating it to the next layer. This approach keeps the volume of text each node receives within consistent bounds. For instance, Du et al. \[[95](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib95)\] recursively aggregate information from neighboring nodes at higher layers and leverage LLMs to rephrase and compress the content before sharing it with relevant nodes in lower layers. Consequently, this strategy expands the receptive field while optimizing the computational budget for downstream generation tasks.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">6.5 </span> Generator

Once the relevant content is retrieved and organized appropriately, it is further processed by a downstream generator to produce the final content. Depending on the desired output, existing generators used for social graphs can be categorized into LLM-based text generators and Prediction-based generators. The choice between these two depends on the format of the desired output and the requirements of the social graph applications.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I5.i1.p1.1.1">LLM-based Text Generation</span>: This generator is typically used when the downstream application requires text outputs \[[510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510), [463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463), [416](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib416), [429](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib429)\], such as item recommendations based on names, recommendation explanations, and review generation. Due to the probabilistic nature of the next token prediction, the generated text may not always precisely align with the desired output. To address this hallucination issue, the ground-truth text is often used for grounding the generated texts, such as matching generated items with the ground-truth items on the platform \[[152](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib152)\].

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I5.i2.p1.1.1">Prediction-based Generation</span>: The generator directly predicts outputs and is primarily used for non-textual tasks \[[95](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib95)\], such as item recommendation and social prediction. For instance, Graph Neural Networks have become popular choices for graph-based recommendation \[[95](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib95), [438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438)\] and transformers are used for item recommendation tasks \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\]. Furthermore, in social property prediction \[[183](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib183)\], the generator could simply be a multilayer perception used for classification (e.g., partisanship classification) or regression (e.g., morality regression).

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">6.6 </span> Resources and Tools

This section lists common resources and tools used for GraphRAG on social graphs. The summary and the link of are itemized as follows:

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">6.6.1 </span> Data Resources

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i1.p1.1.1">STARK-Amazon<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote23"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">23</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">23</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote23.1.1.1">23</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://stark.stanford.edu/dataset_amazon.html" title="">https://stark.stanford.edu/dataset_amazon.html</a></span></span></span></span> \[[452](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib452)\]: STARK-Amazon is a large-scale, semi-structured retrieval benchmark dataset for product search on the Amazon platform, integrating textual and relational knowledge bases. The nodes in the dataset represent products, colors, brands, and categories, while edges capture relationships like also\_bought, also\_viewed, has\_brand, has\_category, and has\_color. Rich textual information, including product descriptions, customer reviews, and entity names, provides valuable context for retrieval tasks. Queries are generated by sampling relational templates and grounding them with specific entities, then leveraging LLMs to synthesize relevant textual and relational information. This process results in queries that capture customer interests, interpret specialized descriptions, and deduce relationships involving multiple entities within the query.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i2.p1.1.1">Amazon-Review<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote24"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">24</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">24</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote24.1.1.1">24</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://jmcauley.ucsd.edu/data/amazon/" title="">https://jmcauley.ucsd.edu/data/amazon/</a></span></span></span></span> \[[306](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib306), [146](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib146), [290](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib290), [463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510)\]: The Amazon-Review dataset includes reviews with ratings, text, and helpfulness votes, along with product metadata such as descriptions, categories, price, brand, and image features. Additionally, it provides link information through “also viewed” and “also bought” graphs. This dataset has two versions: the initial release logging the review data between 1996 and 2014 \[[146](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib146), [290](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib290)\], and a more recent version that logs ongoing data in 2014 \[[306](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib306)\]. The latest version also adds new metadata, including detailed product information, bullet points, and an ethical details table. Extensive GraphRAG work  in social graphs has leveraged this dataset to build RAG frameworks for recommendation research.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i3.p1.1.1">MovieLens<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote25"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">25</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">25</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote25.1.1.1">25</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://grouplens.org/datasets/movielens/" title="">https://grouplens.org/datasets/movielens/</a></span></span></span></span> \[[416](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib416), [438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510)\]: The MovieLens datasets describe people’s expressed preferences for movies \[[143](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib143)\]. These preferences take the form of tuples, each resulting in a person expressing a preference (a 0-5 star rating) for a movie at a particular time. These preferences were entered through the MovieLens website — a recommender system that asks its users to give movie ratings to receive personalized movie recommendations. Side information includes movie title, year, and genre in textual format. \[[438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438)\] further crawl the visual content of movie posters accessible from [here](https://github.com/HKUDS/LLMRec). Note that Movielen-100k is frequently used \[[438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438), [510](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib510)\] and provides users with demographic information such as user ID, age, gender, occupation, and zip code.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i4.p1.1.1">Netflix<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote26"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">26</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">26</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote26.1.1.1">26</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://www.kaggle.com/datasets/netflix-inc/netflix-prize-data" title="">https://www.kaggle.com/datasets/netflix-inc/netflix-prize-data</a></span></span></span></span> \[[438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438)\]: This dataset was constructed to support participants in the Netflix Prize. The movie rating files contain over 100 million ratings from 480 thousand randomly chosen, anonymous Netflix customers over 17 thousand movie titles. The data were collected between October 1998 and December 2005 and reflect the distribution of all ratings received during this period. The date of each rating, the title, and the year of release for each movie ID are also provided. The multi-model side information is collected through web crawling \[[438](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib438)\], which is further stored [here](https://github.com/HKUDS/LLMRec).

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i5.p1.1.1">Yelp<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote27"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">27</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">27</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote27.1.1.1">27</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://www.yelp.com/dataset" title="">https://www.yelp.com/dataset</a></span></span></span></span> \[[463](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib463)\]: The Yelp dataset is a rich resource for academic research, particularly in fields like data science, natural language processing (NLP), and machine learning. This comprehensive dataset includes over 6.9 million reviews, 150,346 businesses, and 200,100 photos, covering 11 metropolitan areas. Researchers and students can explore real-world business and user data, with extensive details about businesses such as location, categories, attributes (like hours, parking, and ambiance), and aggregated check-ins. Reviews include full-text content, user ratings, and metadata, while user profiles offer insights into social interactions and behaviors, including friends, compliments, and review history. Additionally, the dataset contains 908,915 tips, providing quick recommendations and insights, and 1.2 million business attributes, offering granular information about service offerings.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i6.p1.1.1">Weibo<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote28"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">28</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">28</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote28.1.1.1">28</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://www.aminer.cn/influencelocality" title="">https://www.aminer.cn/influencelocality</a></span></span></span></span> \[[521](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib521)\]: The Weibo dataset offers a rich snapshot of user interactions, behaviors and social connections within the Sina Weibo platform, capturing both static and dynamic facets of the social network. Starting with 100 randomly selected seed users, the dataset scales to encompass 1.7 million users and around 0.4 billion following relationships, averaging 200 followers per user. Each user is characterized by social profile details, including name, gender, verification status, and follower/followee counts. Tweet content is provided in both original Chinese and indexed formats, supporting robust research in social network analysis, content diffusion, and user interaction dynamics, making it an invaluable resource for RAG studies.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i7.p1.1.1">Brexit<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote29"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">29</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">29</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote29.1.1.1">29</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://github.com/somethingx01/TopicalAttentionBrexit?tab=readme-ov-file" title="">https://github.com/somethingx01/TopicalAttentionBrexit?tab=readme-ov-file</a></span></span></span></span> \[[565](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib565)\]: This dataset includes a portion of the X (Twitter) network, specifically the remain-leave discourse before the 2016 UK Referendum on exiting the EU. It comprises a network with 7,589 users, 532,459 directed follow relationships, and 19,963 tweets, each associated with a binary stance. The dataset is preprocessed according to \[[298](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib298)\] to assign each user a scalar value between 0 and 1, referred to as opinion, representing the average stance of the tweets retweeted by the user. The stance of each tweet is either 0 (“Remain”) or 1 (“Leave”).

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i8.p1.1.1">Diginetica<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote30"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">30</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">30</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote30.1.1.1">30</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://competitions.codalab.org/competitions/11161#learn_the_details-data2" title="">https://competitions.codalab.org/competitions/11161#learn_the_details-data2</a></span></span></span></span> \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\]: This dataset comprises user session logs from an e-commerce search engine. The data spans six months and captures user interactions, including clicks, product views, and purchases. Each user session, defined by a one-hour inactivity period, contains anonymized user IDs, hashed queries, product descriptions, metadata (price, hashed product names, image identifiers, and product categories), and log-scaled prices.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I6.i9.p1.1.1">Yoochoose<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote31"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">31</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">31</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote31.1.1.1">31</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://www.kaggle.com/datasets/chadgostopp/recsys-challenge-2015" title="">https://www.kaggle.com/datasets/chadgostopp/recsys-challenge-2015</a></span></span></span></span> \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\]: The Yoochoose dataset contains session data from an online European retailer. Each session records the user’s click events, with some sessions also including purchase events. Collected over several months in 2014, the data captures user interactions with the retailer’s website. The product meta-data includes categories.

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">6.6.2 </span> Tools

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I7.i1.p1.1.1">X-Developer Platform<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote32"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">32</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">32</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote32.1.1.1">32</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://developer.x.com/en/docs/x-api/getting-started/about-x-api" title="">https://developer.x.com/en/docs/x-api/getting-started/about-x-api</a></span></span></span></span>: The X Developer Platform provides powerful tools and resources for developers to integrate X’s real-time, historical, and global data into their own applications. With three main products—X API, X Ads API, and X for Websites—the platform supports a wide range of use cases, from retrieving and analyzing Tweets to managing ad campaigns and embedding X content directly into websites. The X API offers endpoints for managing conversations, exploring trends, and engaging with users, while the X Ads API enables businesses to manage ads with custom targeting and analytics. X for Websites allows seamless embedding of live content to enhance website engagement. Through comprehensive documentation, libraries, and community support, the X Developer Platform enables developers to create innovative solutions using X’s data and engagement tools.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I7.i2.p1.1.1">Reddit-API<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote33"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">33</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">33</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote33.1.1.1">33</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://support.reddithelp.com/hc/en-us/articles/16160319875092-Reddit-Data-API-Wiki" title="">https://support.reddithelp.com/hc/en-us/articles/16160319875092-Reddit-Data-API-Wiki</a></span></span></span></span>: Reddit is a news aggregation and discussion platform where posts are organized into "subreddits," user-created boards moderated by the community. The Reddit API provides developers access to the site’s extensive collection of posts and comments. This free API has enabled the development of moderation tools, third-party applications, and training datasets for LLMs such as ChatGPT, Google, and Gemini. By using this API, we can query tremendous user interactions (i.e., comments and posts) and their corresponding textual contents (i.e., subreddit threads), which serve as golden resources for GraphRAG.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S6.I7.i3.p1.1.1">Rec-Bole<span class="ltx_note ltx_role_footnote" id="footnote34"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">34</sup><span class="ltx_note_outer"><span class="ltx_note_content"><sup class="ltx_note_mark">34</sup><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_note"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium" id="footnote34.1.1.1">34</span></span><a class="ltx_ref ltx_href ltx_font_medium" href="https://recbole.io/" title="">https://recbole.io/</a></span></span></span></span> \[[553](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib553)\]: RecBole is an open-source, unified, and comprehensive library designed for developing and benchmarking recommendation algorithms. Built with Python and PyTorch, RecBole offers researchers a streamlined, efficient framework to experiment with over 100 recommendation algorithms across four main types: General, Sequential, Context-Aware, and Knowledge-Based Recommendations. The platform simplifies data handling by providing pre-processed copies of 43 benchmark datasets, making it easy for users to dive into model testing and development. The provided user-product interactions, as well as text-formatted user/product meta-data, could also be used for GraphRAG.

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">7 </span> Planning and Reasoning Graph ![[Uncaptioned image]](extracted/6113564/Figure/logo_plan.png)

A planning or reasoning graph characterizes the inherent logical flow among different entities, where entities typically represent concrete planning or reasoning substeps, and edges denote their logical relations. For the planning graph \[[356](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib356), [569](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib569)\], a common example is a set of API tools used to achieve certain goals, where nodes represent actions, and edges denote their relational dependencies. For reasoning graphs, a notable example is the recent proposed chain/tree/graph of thoughts techniques \[[24](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib24), [437](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib437), [488](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib488)\] where each node represents a decision-making thinking step connected by the reasoning flow. The dependency constraint and reasoning flow in the planning/reasoning graphs can be naturally represented as relational knowledge, which forms the foundation for GraphRAG in fulfilling planning/reasoning tasks. This section reviews GraphRAG for the planning and reasoning graph \[[24](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib24), [248](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib248), [282](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib282), [342](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib342), [345](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib345), [356](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib356), [355](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib355), [368](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib368), [372](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib372), [454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454), [437](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib437), [477](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib477), [488](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib488), [555](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib555), [569](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib569), [142](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib142)\].

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">7.1 </span> Application Tasks

The representative tasks conducted on planning and reasoning graphs are summarized as follows:

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.1">Sequential Plan Retrieval</span> \[[356](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib356), [355](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib355), [372](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib372), [454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454), [555](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib555)\]: As one of the most frequently encountered tasks, plan retrieval aims to retrieve the plan of steps or tools in the format of subgraphs to complete user queries. For example, given the user query "Please generate an image where a girl is reading a book, and her pose is the same as the boy in "example.jpg," then please describe the new image with your voice.", the retrieved final plan from the global plan graph would be "Post Detection" <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1a"><mo id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1" stretchy="false" xref="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml">→</mo><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1.1">→</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1c">\rightarrow</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.1.m1.1d">→</annotation></semantics></math> "Pose-to-Image" <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1a"><mo id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1" stretchy="false" xref="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml">→</mo><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1b"><ci id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1.1">→</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1c">\rightarrow</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.2.m2.1d">→</annotation></semantics></math> "Image-to-Text" <math alttext="\rightarrow" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1a"><mo id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1.1" stretchy="false" xref="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml">→</mo><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1b"><ci id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1.1">→</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1c">\rightarrow</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S7.I1.i1.p1.3.m3.1d">→</annotation></semantics></math> "Text-to-Speech."

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I1.i2.p1.1.1">Naturalistic Asynchronous Planning</span> \[[248](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib248)\]: In contrast to plan retrieval, which considers only dependency constraints among plans, incorporating time constraints introduces a greater challenge. Naturalistic Asynchronous Planning aims to produce a plan that meets dependency requirements and optimizes task completion efficiency, using time summation, time comparison, and constrained reasoning. For example, a user might request, "To make calzones, here are the steps and times required; please calculate the optimal plan for completion." An efficient plan would execute "roll dough," "add filling," and "fill dough" sequentially, with "preheat oven" in parallel, and then conclude with "bake."

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I1.i3.p1.1.1">Structured Commonsense Reasoning</span> \[[342](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib342)\]: Given a belief and an argument, structured common sense reasoning aims to infer the stance and generate/retrieve the corresponding commonsense explanation graph that explains the inferred stance.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I1.i4.p1.1.1">Defeasible Inference</span> \[[282](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib282)\]: Defeasible Inference is a mode of reasoning in which, given a premise, a hypothesis may be weakened or overturned in light of new evidence. A prominent approach is to support defeasible inference through argumentation by constructing inference graphs.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I1.i5.p1.1.1">Tool Usage</span> \[[570](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib570), [142](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib142)\]: Instructing LLMs to use external tools for complex real-world problems has gained increasing importance. Recent research has explored advanced planning strategies to enhance LLMs’ tool-use intelligence. Notably, two approaches employ A\* search \[[570](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib570)\] and Monte Carlo Tree Search \[[142](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib142)\], both utilizing graph-structured reasoning to adaptively retrieve the next tool based on the LLM’s internal evaluations and environmental feedback. These methods enable dynamic tool retrieval, refining the model’s problem-solving precision and flexibility.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I1.i6.p1.1.1">Embodied Planning</span> \[[368](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib368), [477](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib477)\]: Embodied planning tasks in Embodied AI involve guiding agents to perform sequences of actions based on natural language instructions and visual cues in simulated or real-world environments. These tasks, such as organizing or cleaning, challenge agents due to ambiguous instructions, limited task-specific knowledge, sparse feedback, and complex, variable action spaces.

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">7.2 </span> Reasoning and Planning Graph Construction

Most existing methods for constructing reasoning and planning graphs begin by analyzing relational dependencies and subsequently adding edges based on these hard-coded rules. Therefore, rather than limiting our focus to reviewing this only rule-based construction method, we review various dependency categories used for edge addition.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I2.i1.p1.1.1">Resource Dependency</span> \[[355](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib355), [372](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib372), [454](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib454)\]: This dependency is defined as the shared resources among different actions/decisions. For example, two tools are connected if the output of one tool matches the input of the other, enabling a seamless transition from one process to another. The decision to add edges in existing graph construction methods is made by checking whether one node’s input matches another node’s output (e.g., plans, tools, or some other abstract processes).

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I2.i2.p1.1.1">Temporal Dependency</span> \[[355](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib355)\]: This relation ensures that the sequence of events follows certain orders within the planning and reasoning process. For example, connections in some collected datasets denote the successive order between two APIs for daily life.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I2.i3.p1.1.1">Inclusive Dependency</span> \[[277](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib277)\]: The dependency described indicates that two connected nodes belong to the same category or environment. For example, cobblestones and birdhouses are both part of the category of garden decorations \[[427](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib427)\]. Hypergraphs can effectively capture such belonging relationships where one entity belongs to multiple environments \[[111](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib111)\]. Furthermore, these dependencies often form hierarchical structures, where "grandparent" entities encompass "parent" entities, which in turn encompass their "children." As the depth of the hierarchy increases, the space of possible dependencies grows exponentially, presenting a significant computational challenge. To address this, many previous works have proposed encoding such hierarchies in hyperbolic space \[[277](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib277), [257](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib257), [527](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib527)\]. To the best of our knowledge, no prior research has explored inclusive dependencies in RAG systems.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I2.i4.p1.1.1">Causual Dependency</span> \[[248](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib248)\]: This dependency indicates the cause-and-effect logic within the graph, where one action/decision causes the trigger of another action/decision. A long-standing example is the casual graph to encode assumptions about the data-generating process.

*   <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_item">•</span>

    <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" id="S7.I2.i5.p1.1.1">Analogy Dependency</span> \[[499](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib499), [503](https://arxiv.org/html/2501.00309v2#bib.bib503)\]: This dependency underscores analogical reasoning, where relationships take the form "A is to B as C is to D." By recognizing and leveraging such dependency, humans build on existing knowledge to forge new insights across domains.
